Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Taking Compensation in Khul' Based on Nushuz
Ruling with proof on khul' compensation and nushuz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking compensation in khul' depending on which spouse is nashiz and explain the Quranic evidence that supports the distinction.

Core Idea
Taking compensation when the husband is nashiz is haram; when the wife is nashiz it is permissible but excess is discouraged.

The ruling on khul' compensation is not uniform—it hinges on which spouse is at fault. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position that taking money when the husband is nashiz is prohibited (karahah tahrimiyyah), while taking it when the wife is nashiz is permitted, though taking more than the mahr is discouraged. We will examine the Quranic verses and the hadith of Thabit's wife that form the basis of this distinction, and see how the commentators reconcile seemingly conflicting texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: when the husband is nashiz and when the wife is nashiz. Note the different rulings for each.
Nushuz means marital disobedience—harshness from the husband or rebellion from the wife.
2
Examine the ruling for husband's nushuz
The matn says 'makruh' but the commentary clarifies it is karahah tahrimiyyah, effectively haram. Locate the Quranic evidence (al-Nisa 4:20) cited for this.
The prohibition is absolute: 'do not take any of it.'
3
Examine the ruling for wife's nushuz
When the wife is nashiz, taking compensation is not disliked, but the hadith of Thabit's wife restricts taking more than the mahr. Identify the evidence (al-Baqara 2:229 and the hadith).
The phrase 'fala junah' indicates permissibility.
4
Reconcile the two texts
The commentary explains that al-Nisa 4:20 applies when the husband is at fault, and al-Baqara 2:229 applies when the wife is at fault. The hadith adds the boundary on excess.
This reconciliation is a key method in Hanafi usul: applying each text to its specific context.
5
Understand the objection and response
The passage addresses an objection: if taking compensation when husband is nashiz is prohibited, why is the khul' still valid? The answer distinguishes between sinfulness (haram) and validity (sahih).
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a prohibited action can still be legally effective, like selling during the Friday adhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
باب الخلع
وكره له أخذ شيء إن نشز، وإن نشزت لا،
أي كره للزوج تحريماً
شيء
خالعها أو طلقها بخمر أو خنزير أو ميتة وقع بائن في الخلع.
أي المرأة أي الزوج المسلم امرأته
مما ليس بمال
أي طلاق بائن
أي أن يكون
وكره له أخذ شيء إن نشز : [ أي الزوج والحق أن الأخذ في هذه الحالة حرام للنهي القطعي. (ط)] لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَرَدْتُمُ اسْتِبْدَالَ زَوْجٍ مَكَانَ زَوْجِ وَآتَيْتُمْ إِحْدَاهُنَّ قِنْطَاراً فَلَا تَأْخُذُوا مِنْهُ شَيْئًا (النساء:٢٠) ولأنه أوحشها بالفراق، فلا يزيد في إيحاشها بأخذ المال تبيين الحقائق: ١٨٤/٢٢] والمراد بالكراهة التحريمية، ويلحق به الإبراء من صداقها، والنشز بسكون المعجمة أو بالتحرك المكان المرتفع والجمع نشوز وأنشاز، ومنه نشزت المرأة على زوجها إذا استعصت عليه ،وأبغضته ويقال : نشز على زوجته إذا ضربها وجفاها ونشزت المرأة إذا استصعبت على بعلها وجاء نشست و نشصت. عيني، فتح) أخذ شيء له قيمة منها قل أو كثر. (ط) وإن نشزت لا: أي إن كان النشوز من قبلها لا يكره له أخذ العوض إلا أنه يكره أن يأخذ منها أكثر مما أعطاها؛ لقوله لامرأة ثابت بن قيس بن شماس حين أرادت الفرقة: "أتردّين عليه حديقته؟" قالت: نعم وزيادة، فقال عليها: "أما الزيادة فلا" وقد كان النشوز منها، ولو أخذ الزيادة جاز قضاء، وكذا إذا أخذ شيئاً والنشوز منه؛ لأن مقتضى قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا افْتَدَتْ بِهِ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٢٩) الجواز حكماً والإباحة، وقد ترك العمل به في حق الإباحة لمعارض، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلا تَأْخُذُوا مِنْهُ شَيْئًا» (النساء:٢٠) وقوله عليها: "أما الزيادة فلا فبقي معمولا به في الباقي وهو الصحة تبيين الحقائق: ١٨٤/٣]
فإن قيل: النهي للخشية يقتضي عدم المشروعية، فكيف يصح أخذه بعد النهي، قلنا: ورد النهي في غيره وهو زيادة الإيحاش، فلا ينافي المشروعية كالبيع عند النداء، وهذا لأنها تصرفت في خاص حقها باختيارها، فوجب القول بصحته تصحيحاً لتصرف العاقل وتوفيقاً بين النصين. (الزيلعي)
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. And it is disliked for him to take anything if he is nashiz, and if she is nashiz, no. (i.e., disliked for the husband with tahrim; something; she divorces him or he divorces her with wine or pig or carrion, it occurs as ba'in in khul'. i.e., the woman; i.e., the Muslim husband; his wife; from that which is not property; i.e., a ba'in divorce; i.e., that it be.) And it is disliked for him to take anything if he is nashiz: [i.e., the husband, and the truth is that taking in this situation is haram due to the definitive prohibition (T)] due to His saying, the Exalted: 'And if you desire to replace a wife with another and you have given one of them a great amount, do not take any of it...' (al-Nisa 4:20) and because he has saddened her by separation, so he should not increase her sadness by taking wealth. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 2/184. And what is meant by karahah is tahrimi. And included is the remission of her dower. Nashz (with sukun of the shin or with movement) is the elevated place, plural nushuz and anshaz. From it: the woman nashazat against her husband if she rebelled against him and hated him. It is said: he nashaza against his wife if he hit her and was harsh, and she nashazat if she rebelled against her husband. (al-'Ayni, Fath) Taking something of value from her, little or much. (T) 'And if she is nashiz, no': i.e., if the nushuz is from her side, it is not disliked for him to take compensation, except that it is disliked for him to take more than what he gave her; due to his statement to the wife of Thabit b. Qais b. Shammas when she wanted separation: 'Will you return his garden to him?' She said: 'Yes, and more.' So he said to her: 'As for the increase, no.' And the nushuz was from her. If he took the increase, it is valid judicially. Likewise if he took something while the nushuz was from him; because the implication of His statement: 'There is no sin upon them concerning what she ransoms herself with' (al-Baqara 2:229) is permissibility in ruling and permission. And it has been left unacted upon in respect of permission due to a conflicting evidence, which is His statement: 'Do not take any of it' (al-Nisa 4:20) and his statement to her: 'As for the increase, no.' So it remains operative in the rest, which is validity. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/184. If it is said: The prohibition for fear implies lack of legitimacy, so how can its taking be valid after the prohibition? We say: The prohibition came for something else, namely increasing the isolation, so it does not contradict legitimacy, like selling at the time of the call (to prayer). And this is because she acted in her own specific right by her choice, so it is necessary to say it is valid to validate the action of a sane person and to reconcile between the two texts. (al-Zayla'i)
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary explains the ruling on taking compensation in khul'. It first states that it is 'disliked' (makruh) for the husband to take anything if he is nashiz, but the commentators clarify that this is karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike), meaning it is actually haram. The evidence is the Quranic verse (al-Nisa 4:20) prohibiting taking back wealth from a divorced wife, because the husband's nushuz made the separation his fault, so taking money would increase the harm. When the wife is nashiz, it is not disliked for the husband to take compensation, but the hadith of Thabit's wife shows that he should not take more than he gave. The commentary addresses an objection: if the prohibition implies lack of validity, how can taking be valid? The answer is that the prohibition is for a different cause (increasing isolation), and does not affect the validity of the transaction, similar to selling during the Friday adhan—it is a sin but the sale is valid. This shows the Hanafi principle of separating sinfulness from legal validity.

Key Terms
نشز (nashaza / nushuz): Disobedience by either spouse; the husband's harshness or the wife's rebellion against marital duties.
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrimiyyah): A level of dislike that is close to haram; the act is prohibited but proven by a zanni dalil (non-definitive evidence).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling on a husband taking compensation in khul' when he is nashiz?
A) It is permissible (mubah) because the wife agreed.
B) It is forbidden (haram) due to Quranic prohibition.
C) It is disliked (makruh tanzihi) but valid.
D) It is invalid (batil) and the khul' does not occur.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on compensation in khul' depends on nushuz: haram when the husband is at fault, permissible when the wife is at fault, with an additional discouragement on taking more than the mahr.

Valid Compensation in Khul' – Analogy from Mahr
Specifies what can serve as badal in khul' and ruling when compensation is non-property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that any property valid as mahr is also valid as badal in khul', and explain the logical scope of the universal affirmative proposition.

Core Idea
Any valid mahr item is valid khul' compensation, but not conversely.

In this passage, the commentator explains a core principle: the compensation (badal) in khul' must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) just like mahr. However, the relationship is not reciprocal. This lesson unpacks the qiyas argument and its logical boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl and its hukm
Recognize that mahr (dower) is the original case (asl) and is valid with any valuable property (minimum ten dirhams).
The minimum is a Hanafi condition for mahr.
2
Identify the far' and the illa
The far' is the compensation in khul'. The illa (effective cause) is that both compensate the same thing (right to intercourse), but in one case it is valuable (entering ownership) and in the other less valuable (exiting ownership).
3
Apply the qiyas
What is valid for the more valuable case (mahr) is even more suitable for the less valuable case (khul'). Hence, any property valid as mahr is valid as khul' compensation.
This is a qiyas jaliy.
4
Address the objection
Some objects (like less than ten dirhams) are valid in khul' but not as mahr. The reply: the principle is a universal affirmative, not a universal negative. It does not claim that all khul' items are valid as mahr, only that all valid mahr items are valid for khul'.
A common logical confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما صلح إلخ [ أي أن يكون مهراً وهو عشرة دراهم فأكثر. (ط، ع)] أي ما جاز أن يكون بدل المهر جاز أن يكون بدلا في الخلع؛ لأن البضع في حالة الدخول في الملك متقوم، وفي حالة الخروج عن الملك غير متقوم، فما جاز أن يكون عوضاً للمتقوم أولى أن يجوز عوضاً لغير المتقوم، فإن قلت: قد يصلح للخلع ما لا يصلح للمهر كالأقل من العشرة وكما في يدها وبطن غنمها ونحو ذلك، قلنا: إن هذه القضية أي ما صلح مهرا إلخ موجبة كلية، فلا ينعكس موجبة كلية كما تقرر في موضعه؛ إذ لو قلت: كل ما يصلح مهراً لا يصلح بدل الخلع كانت كاذبة، وأما انعكاسها جزئية كقولك: بعض ما صلح بدل خلع يصلح مهرا، والبعض لا يصح. (فتح، مستخلص) وقع بائن في الخلع إلخ أي لو خالع امرأته بما ليس بمال كالخمر والخنزير فلا شيء للزوج، والفرقة بائنة في صورة الخلع، ورجعية في صورة الطلاق على خمر أو خنزير فيقع الطلاق في الوجهين للتعليق بالقبول، وأما الافتراق بالبينونة والرجعية؛ فلأن العوض إذا بطل في الخلع بقي لفظه وهو كناية، والواقع بها بائن، وإذا بطل في الطلاق بقي صريحة وهو يقتضي الرجعة، حتى لو كان بلفظ الكناية يقع بائناً، وإنما لا يجب للزوج شيء؛ لأنها لا تسمّى مالاً متقوماً حتى تصير غارة، بخلاف ما إذا خالعت على خل بعينه فظهر خمراً؛ لأنها سمت مالاً وصار الزوج مغروراً
And 'What is suitable as mahr' etc. [i.e., that it be mahr, which is ten dirhams or more. (T, A)] i.e., whatever is permitted as a substitute for mahr is permitted as a substitute in khul'; because the vulva (right to intercourse) in the state of entry into ownership is valuable, and in the state of exit from ownership is not valuable. So what is permitted as a compensation for the valuable is more fitting to be permitted as compensation for the non-valuable. If you say: There may be suitable for khul' what is not suitable as mahr, like less than ten dirhams, and like that which is in her hand and the belly of her sheep and the like. We say: This proposition 'what is suitable as mahr' etc. is a universal affirmative, so it does not reverse as a universal affirmative, as established in its place; for if you said: Everything suitable as mahr is not suitable as badal of khul' would be false. As for its partial reversal like you say: Some that is suitable as badal of khul' is suitable as mahr, and some is not. (Fath, Mustakhlis) 'It occurs as ba'in in khul'' etc., i.e., if he divorces his wife by khul' with that which is not property, like wine or pig, then there is nothing for the husband, and the separation is ba'in in the form of khul', and raj'i in the form of talaq (divorce) with wine or pig. So the divorce occurs in both ways due to the suspension by acceptance. As for the separation by ba'in and raj'i, because if the compensation is invalid in khul', its wording remains which is kinayah (metaphorical), and what occurs is ba'in. If it is invalid in talaq, it remains explicit (sarih), and that requires raj'a, even if it was by kinayah wording, it occurs as ba'in. And nothing is owed to the husband because she did not name valuable property to become a guarantor (gharra), contrary to the case if she divorced by khul' with a specific vinegar that turned out to be wine; because she named property and the husband became deceived.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This section from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two points: (1) what qualifies as valid compensation (badal) in khul', and (2) the ruling when the compensation is something that is not property (like khamr, khinzir, maytah). On the first, the principle is that any property that can serve as mahr (with a minimum value of ten dirhams in Hanafi fiqh) can be compensation in khul'. The reasoning is qiyas (analogy): since the right to intercourse (bada') is valuable when entering the marriage (i.e., when it is owned by the husband), it is even less valuable when leaving (khul'), so anything that compensates the more valuable case (mahr) is suitable for the less valuable (khul'). However, this is a universal affirmative statement—it does not entail the converse. There are items valid in khul' that are not valid as mahr, e.g., less than ten dirhams or ambiguous items like 'what is in her hand'. The second point: if a khul' is done with something that is not property (like wine or pig), the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) in the case of khul', whereas if the husband simply pronounces talaq with such compensation, it is raj'i (revocable). The difference is due to the nature of the wording: in khul', the utterance is kinayah (allusive) and becomes ba'in when compensation is invalid; in talaq, the utterance is sarih (explicit) and remains effective as a raj'i talaq. The husband does not receive the compensation because the item is not valuable property. If the wife mistakenly names a property that turns out to be invalid (e.g., vinegar that becomes wine), the husband is entitled to something because he was deceived (gharar).

Key Terms
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): The compensation given by the wife to the husband in exchange for her release from the marriage.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a wife offers her husband a sum of 8 dirhams (less than the minimum mahr of 10 dirhams) as compensation for khul'?
A) The khul' is invalid because the compensation is less than the mahr minimum.
B) The khul' is valid and the divorce is ba'in, and the husband receives 8 dirhams.
C) The khul' is valid but the husband must return the 8 dirhams because it is not proper compensation.
D) The khul' is valid only if the husband agrees to accept less than ten dirhams.

Key Takeaway
Any valuable property, even below the mahr minimum, is valid as compensation in khul' because the right being compensated is less valuable in exit than in entry.

Non-Property Compensation: Khul' vs Talaq
Specifies what can serve as badal in khul' and ruling when compensation is non-property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling for non-property compensation (like wine/pig) in khul' versus talaq, and explain why the difference arises from the nature of the wording.

Core Idea
Khul' with non-property gives ba'in divorce; talaq with non-property gives raj'i divorce.

The second part of the passage addresses a critical distinction: what happens when the wife offers something that is not property (like wine or pig) as compensation for khul'? The ruling differs from a talaq exchange with the same non-property item. This lesson explores the reasoning based on whether the divorce wording is kinayah (in khul') or sarih (in talaq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: Khul' with non-property (e.g., khamr). Scenario B: Talaq with non-property (e.g., khamr).
2
State the ruling for each
Scenario A: Divorce is ba'in, husband receives nothing. Scenario B: Divorce is raj'i, husband receives nothing.
3
Analyse the reasoning
In khul', the wording is kinayah (allusive). When compensation is invalid, the kinayah alone still effects divorce, but only ba'in. In talaq, the wording is sarih (explicit). Even if compensation is invalid, the sarih talaq remains effective as raj'i.
The text adds: if talaq was spoken in kinayah, it would be ba'in.
4
Understand the exception for gharar
If the wife named a specific property (e.g., vinegar) that turned out to be invalid (wine), the husband is deceived and becomes entitled to something (gharar).
This is a gharar (fraud) case, not the default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما صلح إلخ [ أي أن يكون مهراً وهو عشرة دراهم فأكثر. (ط، ع)] أي ما جاز أن يكون بدل المهر جاز أن يكون بدلا في الخلع؛ لأن البضع في حالة الدخول في الملك متقوم، وفي حالة الخروج عن الملك غير متقوم، فما جاز أن يكون عوضاً للمتقوم أولى أن يجوز عوضاً لغير المتقوم، فإن قلت: قد يصلح للخلع ما لا يصلح للمهر كالأقل من العشرة وكما في يدها وبطن غنمها ونحو ذلك، قلنا: إن هذه القضية أي ما صلح مهرا إلخ موجبة كلية، فلا ينعكس موجبة كلية كما تقرر في موضعه؛ إذ لو قلت: كل ما يصلح مهراً لا يصلح بدل الخلع كانت كاذبة، وأما انعكاسها جزئية كقولك: بعض ما صلح بدل خلع يصلح مهرا، والبعض لا يصح. Commentary
This section from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two points: (1) what qualifies as valid compensation (badal) in khul', and (2) the ruling when the compensation is something that is not property (like khamr, khinzir, maytah). However, this is a universal affirmative statement—it does not entail the converse. There are items valid in khul' that are not valid as mahr, e.g., less than ten dirhams or ambiguous items like 'what is in her hand'. The second point: if a khul' is done with something that is not property (like wine or pig), the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) in the case of khul', whereas if the husband simply pronounces talaq with such compensation, it is raj'i (revocable). The difference is due to the nature of the wording: in khul', the utterance is kinayah (allusive) and becomes ba'in when compensation is invalid; in talaq, the utterance is sarih (explicit) and remains effective as a raj'i talaq. The husband does not receive the compensation because the item is not valuable property. If the wife mistakenly names a property that turns out to be invalid (e.g., vinegar that becomes wine), the husband is entitled to something because he was deceived (gharar).

Key Terms
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): The compensation given by the wife to the husband in exchange for her release from the marriage.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a husband pronounces talaq (sarih) in exchange for a bottle of wine?
A) The talaq is ba'in and the husband gets the wine.
B) The talaq is raj'i and the husband gets nothing.
C) The talaq is invalid because wine is not property.
D) The talaq is ba'in and the husband gets nothing.

Key Takeaway
Khul' with non-property yields a ba'in divorce with no compensation; talaq with non-property yields a raj'i divorce with no compensation; the difference stems from the wording being kinayah vs sarih.

Khul' with Uncertain Compensation: Hanafi Rulings
Ruling on khul' with uncertain compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' when the wife offers unspecified compensation and determine the exact compensation owed when she specifies property or dirhams.

Core Idea
The wife's vague promise in khul' is binding only if she actually has the means; otherwise, the divorce is free—unless she deceives by naming property.

When a wife initiates khul' by saying 'divorce me in return for what is in my hand', the husband may consent without knowing exactly what she has. This lesson examines two scenarios: one where she has nothing and the divorce occurs gratuitously, and another where she mentions property or dirhams but has none, triggering a obligation to return the mahr. The Hanafi school relies on linguistic analysis of the word 'min' and the principle that the vulva is not valuable upon exit to determine the correct compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: wife says 'khali'ni on what is in my hand' with nothing. Second: she adds 'from property' or 'from dirhams' but has nothing. Distinguish the ruling for each.
2
Understand the linguistic role of 'min'
Determine whether 'min' indicates partitivity (tab'id) or clarification (bayan al-jins). The Hanafi position holds it is for clarification, not partiality, so the plural does not impose three dirhams.
This is the key to rejecting the Shafi'i view.
3
Apply the principle of gharar (deception)
When the wife names property, she deceives the husband; he would not have consented without compensation. Therefore, she must return what she actually received from him (the mahr).
If she took no mahr, nothing is owed.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the wife must pay mahr al-mithl and three dirhams if she said 'dirhams'. Understand why Hanafis reject this: the vulva is not valuable upon exit (when khul' occurs), so mahr al-mithl is not applicable.
5
Formulate the complete ruling
State the mufta bihi: if wife says 'khali'ni on what is in my hand' and has nothing → divorce is free. If she adds 'mal' or 'darahim' → she returns the mahr she took. If she took none, nothing.
This is the operative Hanafi fatwa.

It is answered that this is when specification by covenant is not possible, i.e., when there is no evidence indicating the covenant, and it exists here which is her statement 'on what is in my hand', so it is not for the genus, so the plural must be considered. Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This portion of the commentary deals with two scenarios related to khul' where the wife offers an uncertain compensation. The first scenario: the wife says 'khali'ni 'ala ma fi yadi' (divorce me by khul' on what is in my hand) without specifying any amount, and she actually has nothing in her hand. According to the Hanafi school, the divorce occurs (ba'in) completely without any compensation (majanan), because the word 'ma' is general and does not specifically promise property, so there is no deception (gharar). The second scenario: the wife adds a specification, such as 'min al-mal' (from property) or 'min al-darahim' (from dirhams), but she has no such property. In this case, she must return the mahr that she had taken from her husband at the time of marriage, because she deceived him (gharara) by mentioning property. The husband would not have agreed to the khul' without compensation, so the wife must give back what she received. If she had not taken any mahr, nothing is owed. The commentary also discusses the Shafi'i opinion that she must pay mahr al-mithl and three dirhams (if she said 'dirhams') because the plural implies at least three. The Hanafi response includes a linguistic analysis of the word 'min'—whether it indicates partitivity (tab'id) or clarification (bayan al-jins). The conclusion is that here it is for clarification, so the plural (three or more) is considered, but the compensation is limited to the mahr she actually took, not three dirhams. The passage also addresses an objection about the definite form of 'al-darahim' (the dirhams) and explains that the context of 'ma fi yadi' provides a covenant (ahd) so the plural is maintained.

Key Terms
مجاناً (majānan): Gratuitously, without any compensation.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception; the wife leading the husband to believe she will pay compensation when she has none.
من (min): The preposition min used here to specify the genus (bayan al-jins) or for partitivity (tab'id). The commentary determines it is for clarification based on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife says 'Khali'ni 'ala ma fi yadi min al-mal' and has no property. However, she had received a mahr of 200 dirhams at marriage. According to the Hanafi school, what must she do?
A) The divorce does not occur because she deceived.
B) She must return the 200 dirhams mahr.
C) She must pay mahr al-mithl of 300 dirhams.
D) She must pay 200 dirhams plus 3 dirhams for the plural.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a wife's vague promise in khul' binds her only to return what she actually received from the husband, not speculative amounts like mahr al-mithl or a minimum plural.

Khul' for Free and the Effect of Specifying Property
Ruling on khul' being raj'i except with kinaya, and cases of unspecified/specified property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' when the wife specifies property or not, and explain why the return of the mahr is wajib in cases of deception (gharur).

Core Idea
Specifying property in khul' binds the wife to return the mahr she received; without specification, nothing is owed.

This passage from the chapter of khul' (divorce by mutual consent with compensation) explores two key scenarios: when the wife simply says 'on what is in my hand' without specifying, and when she specifies 'property' or 'dirhams' but has nothing. The central legal distinction is whether the husband was deceived (maghrur) into consenting to divorce expecting compensation. The Hanafi ruling uses qiyas to analogize to cases of deception in contracts, requiring the wife to restore what she actually took—the mahr—rather than the named item or mahr al-mithl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage contrasts two cases: (1) wife says 'khali'ni on what is in my hand' with nothing in her hand — the divorce occurs for free (majan). (2) wife says 'on what is in my hand from property or dirhams' and has nothing — she must return the mahr she received.
The word 'ma' in the first scenario is general and does not specify valuable property; thus no deception.
2
Understand the concept of gharur (deception)
When the wife specifies property, she leads the husband to believe he will receive compensation. If she has no such property, she has deceived him (gharur). The husband consented to divorce only expecting compensation, so the wife must indemnify him.
Deception is the basis for recourse; without specification, the husband freely consented without expectation.
3
Analyze why the received mahr is returned
The author argues against obliging the named item (unknown), its value (also unknown), or mahr al-mithl (vulva not valuable upon exit). The only determinate compensation is the mahr the wife actually received from the husband, which is what the husband lost. If she never received it, nothing is owed.
This qiyas is based on the principle that deception obliges restoration of what was lost, not a speculative value.
4
Note the Shafi'i counter-position
Al-Shafi'i holds that she returns mahr al-mithl and, if she specified 'dirhams', at least three dirhams (minimum of plural). This differs from the Hanafi view because al-Shafi'i considers the vulba valuable at exit and treats the phrase as obliging a minimal ascertainable amount.
The Hanafi rebuttal is that the vulva is not valuable (muttaqawwim) at the time of exit, so qiyas on mahr al-mithl fails.
5
Examine the linguistic argument about 'min'
A potential objection: 'min' is partitive, so she should owe only some dirhams (one or two). The Hanafi response: 'min' here is for clarification of genus (li bayan al-jins), not partitive, because the wife's statement 'on what is in my hand' is complete but ambiguous about the type of property. Thus, when she says 'min dirhams', the plural indicates at least three.
If 'dirhams' were definite, the lām of genus could annul plurality, but the context of 'on what is in my hand' indicates a specific thing (ahdiyyah), preserving plurality.

In every place where the speech is complete by itself but contains a type of ambiguity, it is for clarification. And her saying 'khali'ni on what is in my hand' is speech complete by itself except that there is a type of ambiguity because what is in her hand is not known of which genus it is, so it is determined for clarification. And there is no difference between her mentioning the dirhams indefinite or definite. And it was objected that this is apparent in the indefinite; as for the definite, one dirham should be obligatory due to annulment of plurality by the lām of genus. And it was answered that this is when there is no possibility of specification (al-ahdiyyah) i.e. when there is no context indicating specification. And it has been found here, which is her saying 'on what is in my hand', so it is not for genus, so the consideration of plurality is obligatory. (Fath, Mukhtasar) 'Her mahr which she took from him in her saying 'from' 'property, and if she did not receive it, nothing on her.' (T, A)
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of khul' (divorce by mutual consent with compensation) discusses the legal classification of khul' and the consequences of the wife offering unspecified or specified property. The passage begins by stating that khul' is a raj'i divorce (revocable) unless it is pronounced using kinaya (ambiguous expressions), in which case it is ba'in (irrevocable). The author then addresses the scenario where a wife says 'khali'ni on what is in my hand' (i.e., divorce me by khul' in exchange for whatever I possess) but actually has nothing. In this case, the divorce occurs for free (majan) and the wife owes nothing, because the word 'ma' is general and does not specify valuable property, so the husband was not deceived. However, if the wife specifies 'mal' (property) or 'dirhams' and it turns out she has nothing, she must return the dower (mahr) she had previously received from her husband. This is because she deceived him by making him expect property; the husband would not have consented to divorce without compensation. The obligation is not the named item or its value (due to unknownness) nor the mahr al-mithl (because the vulva is not considered valuable upon exiting the marriage), but rather the actual mahr she received. If she never received the mahr, she owes nothing. The Shafi'i position is also noted: she would owe mahr al-mithl and, in the case of specifying 'dirhams', at least three dirhams because the plural minimum is three. A debate follows concerning the word 'min': one might think 'min' implies partitive, requiring one or two dirhams, but the Hanafi scholar argues it is for clarification of genus (li bayan al-jins). The wife's statement 'on what is in my hand' is complete but ambiguous as to the type of property, so 'min' clarifies the genus. The objection that definite 'dirhams' should imply one dirham due to the lām of genus is answered by the presence of a context indicating specification (al-ahdiyyah), namely the phrase 'on what is in my hand', which points to a specific thing. Thus, the consideration of plurality (three) stands. The Hanafi position here, from the Zahir al-Riwayah, is that the wife returns the mahr she received; this is the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife at marriage; in this context, the specific dower that was paid and is now returned upon khul' under certain conditions.
غرور (gharur): Deception; here, the wife deceives the husband by leading him to believe she has valuable property, causing him to consent to khul'.
من (min): A preposition that can indicate partitivity or specification (li bayan al-jins). In this passage, it is argued to be for clarification of genus, not partitive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a wife says 'khali'ni on what is in my hand from property' but she has nothing, and she had received a mahr of 500 dirhams, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) She owes nothing because 'ma' is general.
B) She must return the mahr she received (500 dirhams).
C) She must pay mahr al-mithl and three dirhams.
D) She must pay the value of the property she specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a wife specifies property in khul' but has nothing, she must return the mahr she actually received; unspecified offers result in a free divorce.

Khul' on an Absconding Slave with an Invalid Condition
Ruling on khul' with an absconding slave as compensation, with a condition of exemption from delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' using an absconding slave as compensation and explain why an invalid condition exempting delivery is voided without nullifying the contract.

Core Idea
In khul', an invalid condition is voided but the contract stands; the wife must deliver the slave or its value.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in khul' where the wife offers an absconding slave as compensation but attempts to exempt herself from delivery. The Hanafi school holds that such a condition is invalid, yet the khul' remains valid. Understanding this distinction requires analyzing the nature of khul' as a contract of exchange and its comparison to sale and marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the invalid condition
The khul' is valid despite the absconding slave; the condition exempting the wife from delivery is fasid (invalid).
The condition contradicts the requirement of exchange (mu'awadah).
2
Trace the proof via qiyas
Khul' is analogized to nikah, where invalid conditions are voided but do not nullify the contract, unlike sale.
Sale of an absconding slave is invalid due to gharar and prohibition of invalid conditions.
3
Determine the consequence of the void condition
The condition becomes void, so the wife must deliver the slave if possible, otherwise pay its value.
Exemption from defects is valid, but not from delivery.
4
Note the cross-madhhab contrast
Other schools may treat khul' like sale, but Hanafi distinguishes based on the nature of the contract and prohibition of conditions.
This shows the importance of usul in deriving rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Or three dirhams, and if he divorced her by khul' on an absconding slave belonging to her, on the condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free. (T) i.e., and if he differed [with her] on her absconding slave on the condition that she is free from its delivery and production, the woman is not free; because it is a contract of exchange, it requires soundness of the compensation. Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage deals with khul' using an absconding slave (abd abiq) as the compensation (badal). The wife offers the slave with a condition that she is free from any liability for its delivery (daman). The ruling is that she is not free from that responsibility; the condition is invalid but does not nullify the khul' itself. This is because khul' is a contract of exchange (mu'awadah) which requires the compensation to be deliverable. The condition exempting the wife from delivery contradicts the very purpose of exchange, so it is a fasid (corrupt) condition. However, unlike in sale (bay'), where such a condition would invalidate the entire contract (since sale of an absconding item is invalid and prohibited), in khul' the contract remains valid and the condition is simply voided. The wife must then deliver the slave if she can; otherwise, she must pay its value. Importantly, the exemption from defects ('uyub) is valid, meaning she is not responsible for hidden defects of the slave. This distinction between khul' and sale regarding invalid conditions is based on the principle that khul' is not prohibited with such conditions (nahyi) whereas sale is. The passage also includes a brief mention of the 'three dirhams' case (a return to the earlier discussion) and then moves to the slave case. The Hanafi position here is that the condition of exemption from delivery is void, but the khul' is valid and the wife must deliver the slave or its value. This is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
عبد آبق (abd abiq): A slave who has run away and is not present at the time of contract.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability; here, the responsibility for delivering the slave.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): An invalid condition that contradicts the essence of the contract but does not necessarily invalidate the entire contract in khul'.

D) The khul' is valid, the condition is valid, and she is not liable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khul' remains valid even with an invalid condition; the condition is voided and the wife must deliver the compensation.

The 'Talliqni' Transaction: Condition or Price?
Ruling on deferred divorce with 'bi-alf' vs 'ala-alf'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'ala' in deferred divorce and distinguish the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
With 'ala', Abu Hanifa holds it a condition, so no payment; Sahibayn hold it a price, so one-third owed.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over a wife's request for divorce with compensation using different prepositions. The central question is whether 'ala' (on) indicates a condition, triggering a simple raj'i divorce, or a price, making the divorce ba'in with a payment due. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your grasp of Hanafi usul, particularly the role of ta'liq (suspension) in differentiating divorce from sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
The wife says 'talliqni thalathan 'ala alf' and the husband gives only one divorce. The dispute is about the legal effect: Is the divorce raj'i or ba'in? Does she owe payment?
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its basis
Abu Hanifa rules that the one divorce is raj'i with no payment. His evidence: the Quranic verse (60:12) uses 'ala' for a condition; divorce can be suspended (ta'liq) on a condition; the conditioned (divorce) is not distributed over parts of the condition.
Quran 60:12: 'On that they do not associate...'
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and its basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the one divorce is ba'in and she owes one-third of the thousand. They analogize 'ala' to 'bi' in exchanges, because in sales both prepositions indicate price; since sale cannot be suspended, 'ala' must indicate a price here.
Sahibayn are joined by al-Shafi'i.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school prefers Abu Hanifa's view because ta'liq is valid in divorce but not in sale, so there is no necessity to interpret 'ala' as price. The absence of necessity means 'ala' retains its conditional meaning.
Mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the wife pays nothing and the divorce is raj'i (revocable); under the Sahibayn, she pays one-third and the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable).
This affects the possibility of reconciliation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت "طلقني ثلاثاً بألف" فطلق واحدة له ثلث الألف وبانت، وفي "على" وقع مجانا رجعي. قالت "طلقني ثلاثاً بألف" فطلق واحدة له ثلث الألف وبانت، وفي "على" وقع مجانا رجعي. أي المرأة لزوجها أي ثلاث طلقات أي بألف درهم أي طلقة واحدة أي للزوج مجانا رجعي. طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً بألف أو على فطلقت واحدة . أي طلاق رجعي أي من غير شيء عليه أي ثلاث طلقات أي ألف أي المرأة نفسها أي طلقة واحدة. وقع رجعي. له ثلث الألف [وهو ثلاث مائة وثلاثة وثلاثون درهما وثلث درهم (مسكين)] لأنها لما طلبت ثلاث طلقات بالألف، فقد طلبت كل واحدة بثلث الألف؛ لأن الباء تصحب الأعواض، والعوض ينقسم على المعوّض، وهذا إذا طلقها في المجلس حتى لو قام فطلقها لا يجب شيء ويكون بائناً لوجوب المال إذا لم يكن طلقها قبل قولها له: "طلقني ثلاثاً بألف"، فإن كان طلقها واحدة له كل الألف كما لو طلقها ثلاثاً دفعة أو متفرقة في مجلس واحد، فعند مالك إن طلبت ثلاثاً وطلق واحداً لزمها كل الألف، وعند أحمد يقع الطلاق بغير شيء. (مستخلص، عيني، وفتح) وفي على أي في قولها: طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف فطلقها واحدة. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۷۸/۱] مجاناً: أي لو قالت طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف، فطلقها واحدة يقع رجعيا بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن "على" للشرط عند الإمام قال الله تعالى: ﴿ عَلَى أَنْ لا يُشْرِكْنَ بِاللَّهِ شَيْئاً (الممتحنة: ١٢) الآية، لامرأته: أنت طالق على أن تدخلي الدار يكون شرطاً، والطلاق يصح تعليقه بالشرط، والمشروط لا يتوزع على أجزاء الشرط، وههنا جاز إيقاع الثلاث شرطا للزوم الألف، والبدل لا يوزع على أجزاء الشرط، فيقع رجعية بلا شيء، وعندهما وهو قول الشافعي حاله يقع تطليقة بائنة بثلث الألف؛ لأن "على" مثل الباء في المعاوضات عبداً بألف أو على ألف وله أن البيع لا يصح تعليقه بالشرط، فيحمل على العوض ضرورة، ولا ضرورة في الطلاق لصحة تعليقه بالشرط، فإذا لم يجب المال كان الزوج مبتدئا فيقع رجعيا، وعند مالك: يلزمها كل الألف. (If a man says to his wife: 'You are divorced on the condition that you enter the house', it is a condition, and divorce is valid when suspended on a condition.) And the conditioned is not distributed over parts of the condition. Here it is permissible to make the occurrence of three divorces a condition for the obligation of the thousand, and the compensation is not distributed over parts of the condition, so it occurs raj'i without anything. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and this is also the view of al-Shafi'i, it occurs as one ba'in divorce for one-third of the thousand, because 'ala' is like 'ba' in exchanges, e.g., selling a slave for a thousand or on a thousand. But sale cannot be suspended on a condition, so it is construed as a compensation necessarily. And there is no necessity in divorce because it can be suspended on a condition. So if the payment is not obligatory, the husband becomes the initiator, so it occurs raj'i. According to Malik, the entire thousand is incumbent on her. (Mukhtasar, Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses two classic scenarios involving a wife's request for a compensatory divorce: (1) she says 'talliqni thalathan bi-alf' (divorce me three times with one thousand) and the husband gives only one divorce; (2) she says 'talliqni thalathan 'ala alf' (divorce me three times on one thousand) and he gives one divorce. For the first case, using the preposition 'bi' (with), the divorce is treated as a sale-like exchange: the wife offered one thousand for three divorces, so each divorce is worth one-third. Thus, if the husband gives only one divorce, he is entitled to one-third of the thousand (333.33 dirhams) and the divorce is ba'in. For the second case, using the preposition 'ala' (on), three different opinions are presented. According to Abu Hanifa, 'ala' indicates a condition (like a conditional statement), not an immediate exchange. The wife made the three divorces a condition for the obligation of the thousand. Since it is a condition, the divorce is not tied to the payment immediately; if the husband gives one divorce, it is not considered part of the exchange. Therefore, the divorce occurs as a simple raj'i divorce without any payment. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with al-Shafi'i, hold that 'ala' is similar to 'bi' in the context of compensation, referring to the sale analogy (i.e., 'a slave for a thousand or on a thousand'). Since sale cannot be suspended on a condition, 'ala' must be interpreted as indicating a price. Thus, the wife owes one-third of the thousand and the divorce is ba'in. The text then mentions the views of Malik (entire thousand due) and Ahmad (nothing due) for completeness. The discussion also notes that if the husband divorces her after leaving the council, different rules apply. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the two companions (Sahibayn), with the Hanafi school favoring the Imam's view in this context. The mufta bihi is that with 'ala', the divorce occurs raj'i for free, as stated in the text: 'yqa'u raj'iyyan bi-la shay' 'inda Abi Hanifa'.

Key Terms
باء (ba'): A preposition indicating price/compensation in exchange transactions.
على (ala): A preposition that can indicate either price or condition; the dispute centers on its meaning in this context.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect on a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three divorces occur, and she owes the full thousand

Key Takeaway
The use of 'ala' in a wife's request for divorce creates a conditional suspension, not an immediate exchange, resulting in a raj'i divorce with no payment under the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Khul' with an Absconding Slave: Invalid Condition Does Not Void the Contract
Ruling on validity of immunity condition in khul' with absconding slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a khul' contract involving an absconding slave with a condition of immunity from guarantee, and explain why the condition is void while the khul' remains valid.

Core Idea
An invalid condition in khul' does not void the contract; only the condition is void, unlike sale.

In this passage, we examine a specific scenario in khul': the husband divorces his wife in exchange for a slave that has run away, and she stipulates that she is not responsible for the slave's delivery. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that differentiates khul' from ordinary sale contracts. Understanding this ruling clarifies the nature of khul' as a hybrid of talaq and exchange, and explains why invalid conditions in khul' do not invalidate the entire contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the condition
What is the khul' contract in this case? What specific condition does the wife stipulate?
The compensation is an absconding slave whose location is unknown. The condition is that the wife is immune from any guarantee (i.e., not responsible for delivering the slave or its value).
2
Determine the ruling on the condition
Is the condition valid or void? Why? The text states it is 'fāsid' (invalid) because it contradicts the requirement of a contract of exchange (mu‘āwaḍa) that the consideration be sound and deliverable.
3
Explain the effect on the khul' contract
Does the invalid condition invalidate the entire khul'? No, the khul' remains valid. The text compares khul' to marriage, where invalid conditions do not void the contract, and contrasts it with sale, where they do.
The key distinction: invalid conditions are prohibited in sale (making the sale void) but not in khul'.
4
Analyze the analogy
Why is khul' treated like marriage and unlike sale? Because khul' is fundamentally a form of talaq (dissolution of marriage), which has leniency, whereas sale is a pure exchange contract with stricter requirements.
5
Identify the practical consequence
What must the wife do? She must deliver the slave itself if she can, or pay its value if she cannot. The condition of immunity is void, so she remains liable.
Additionally, the text notes that immunity from defects (not from guarantee) would be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ثلاثة دراهم وإن خالعها على عبد آبق لها على أنها بريئة من ضمانه لم تبرأ، أي المرأة من الضمان وعليها تسليم عينه إن قدرت عليه وقيمته إن عجزت؛ لأنه عقد معاوضة، فيقتضي سلامة العوض، واشتراط البراءة عنه شرط فاسد فيبطل الشرط لكونه مخالفا لموجب العقد ولا يبطل الخلع؛ لأنه لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالنكاح بخلاف البيع حيث لا يصح في الآبق، ويبطل بالشروط الفاسدة؛ لأنه منهي عنها في البيع لا في الخلع، فإذا بطل الشرط وجب عليها تسليم عينه إن قدرت عليه وإلا تسليم قيمته إن عجزت، أما البراءة من عيبه فصحيحة.
Or three dirhams. And if he divorced her by khul' for an absconding slave belonging to her, on condition that she is immune from its guarantee, she is not immune. That is, the woman is not immune from the guarantee, and upon her is delivery of its physical entity if she is able, and its value if she is unable; because it is a contract of exchange, so it requires soundness of the consideration, and stipulating immunity from it is an invalid condition, so the condition is void because it contradicts the requirement of the contract, and the khul' is not void because it is not invalidated by invalid conditions, like marriage, unlike sale where it is not valid for an absconding slave, and is invalidated by invalid conditions; because [invalid conditions] are prohibited in sale, not in khul'. So when the condition is void, delivery of its physical entity is obligatory upon her if she is able, otherwise delivery of its value if she is unable. As for immunity from its defect, it is valid.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the chapter of khul'. It addresses a scenario where a husband divorces his wife by khul' in exchange for a slave that has absconded (run away), with a condition that the wife is immune from any responsibility regarding the slave's delivery. The ruling is that such a condition is invalid; the wife is not immune. She must deliver the slave itself if she can, and if she cannot, she must pay its value. The reasoning is that khul' is a contract of exchange (mu'awada), which inherently requires that the consideration (the slave) be delivered and free from encumbrances. Stipulating immunity from this guarantee contradicts the nature of the contract, making the condition void. However, unlike in a sale contract, an invalid condition does not invalidate the entire khul' contract, because sale is strictly prohibited from containing invalid conditions, whereas khul' is more lenient, similar to marriage. The author also distinguishes between immunity from guarantee (invalid) and immunity from defects (valid); warranty against defects is permissible.

Key Terms
خلع (khul‘): A divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually money) paid to the husband.
عبد آبق (‘abd ābiq): A slave that has run away (absconded) and whose location is unknown.
براءة من ضمان (barā’at min ḍamān): Immunity from guarantee; here meaning the wife is not responsible for the slave's delivery or its value.
عقد معاوضة (‘aqd mu‘āwaḍa): A contract of exchange where each party gives something in return for something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a husband divorces his wife by khul‘ in exchange for an absconding slave, on condition that the wife is immune from any guarantee regarding the slave, what is the ruling?
A) The khul‘ is invalid because the condition is void.
B) The khul‘ is valid and the wife is immune from guarantee.
C) The khul‘ is valid but the condition is void; the wife must deliver the slave or its value.
D) The khul‘ is valid but the husband must pay for the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an invalid condition in khul' does not void the contract; only the condition is void, and the wife must deliver the compensation or its value.

Wife Requests Three Divorces for 1000 Dirhams: Proportionate Compensation
Ruling on compensation in khul' with partial performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation owed when a wife requests multiple divorces for a lump sum and the husband gives fewer, applying the Hanafi principle of proportional apportionment.

Core Idea
Each divorce carries a proportional share of the compensation; giving one entitles him to one third of the agreed sum.

When a wife offers a lump sum for multiple divorces, the Hanafi school treats each divorce as a separate transaction. This lesson unpacks the ruling using a textual example, highlighting the role of the preposition 'ba' in dividing compensation and the conditions that affect liability. You will also see how other schools differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Hanafi Ruling
State the facts: wife says 'Divorce me three times for 1000 dirhams', husband divorces her once. The Hanafi ruling: he is entitled to one third of 1000, and she becomes ba'in.
The amount is exactly 333.33 dirhams.
2
Understand the Linguistic Proof (Dalil)
The letter 'ba' (for) in 'balf' (for 1000) indicates that each divorce is compensated separately. Since the compensation is divisible, it is divided over the number of divorces requested.
This is a core Hanafi usul principle: 'al-ba' li al-iwad' (the ba is for compensation).
3
Note the Conditions and Exceptions
The ruling applies only if the husband divorces in the same session (majlis). If he delays, no compensation is due, though the divorce is still ba'in. Also, if he had already divorced her once before her request, the entire 1000 is due.
These conditions prevent abuse of the khul' offer.
4
Compare with Other Schools
Imam Malik holds the full 1000 is due because the contract was for the entire release. Imam Ahmad holds no payment is due as the condition was not met.
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت 'طلقني ثلاثاً بألف' فطلق واحدة له ثلث الألف وبانت، [وهو ثلاث مائة وثلاثة وثلاثون درهما وثلث درهم] لأنها لما طلبت ثلاث طلقات بالألف، فقد طلبت كل واحدة بثلث الألف؛ لأن الباء تصحب الأعواض، والعوض ينقسم على المعوض، وهذا إذا طلقها في المجلس حتى لو قام فطلقها لا يجب شيء ويكون بائناً لوجوب المال إذا لم يكن طلقها قبل قولها له: 'طلقني ثلاثاً بألف'، فإن كان طلقها واحدة له كل الألف كما لو طلقها ثلاثاً دفعة أو متفرقة في مجلس واحد، فعند مالك إن طلبت ثلاثاً وطلق واحداً لزمها كل الألف، وعند أحمد يقع الطلاق بغير شيء.
She said, 'Divorce me three times for 1000,' so he divorced her once. He is entitled to one third of a thousand, and she becomes ba'in. That is three hundred and thirty-three dirhams and one third of a dirham, because when she requested three divorces for a thousand, she requested each one for one third of a thousand; because the letter 'ba' ('for') accompanies the compensations, and the compensation is divided over the thing compensated. This (applies) if he divorced her in the session; if he stood up and then divorced her, nothing is owed and she becomes ba'in due to the obligation of wealth if he had not divorced her before her saying that. If he had divorced her once earlier, then he gets the entire thousand, just as if he divorced her three times at once or separately in one session. According to Malik, if she requested three and he divorced one, all of the thousand is owed by her. According to Ahmad, the divorce falls without anything.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a wife asks her husband for three divorces in exchange for 1000 dirhams. The husband divorces her only once. According to the Hanafi school, the husband is entitled to one third of the 1000 dirhams (333.33 dirhams) because each divorce is considered a separate compensation. The divorce becomes ba'in (irrevocable). The ruling is conditional: it applies only if the husband divorces her immediately in the same session; if he delays, no compensation is due, and the divorce is still ba'in but the wife owes nothing. If the husband had already divorced her once before her request, then the entire 1000 is due. The text also notes the opinions of Imam Malik and Ahmad: Malik says she owes all 1000, while Ahmad says the divorce occurs without any payment. The Hanafi position is the one stated first.

Key Terms
باء (bā'): The preposition 'bi' meaning 'for' or 'in exchange for' in the context of compensation.
بائن (bā'in): A divorce that is irrevocable; the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
مجلس (majlis): The session or meeting where the divorce offer is made and accepted; immediacy is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1000 dirhams
B) Nothing
C) 333.33 dirhams
D) 500 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a wife offers a lump sum for multiple divorces, each divorce carries a proportional share of the compensation, and the transaction is valid only if concluded immediately in the same session.

The Effect of Wording in Khul': 'Ala' (Upon) vs. 'Bi' (For)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'ala' in khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the two companions' rulings when the wife uses 'ala' in a khul' request, and apply the distinction between 'ala' as a condition vs. exchange.

Core Idea
When a wife says 'ala' (upon), Abu Hanifa sees a condition; the two companions see an exchange.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of the preposition 'ala' (upon) in a wife's request for khul'. The ruling changes dramatically depending on whether 'ala' is treated as a condition (shart) or as a direct exchange (mu'awada). Imam Abu Hanifa, citing a Quranic verse, argues that 'ala' indicates a condition, so the divorce is raj'i and no compensation is due if the husband gives only one divorce. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it like 'bi' in exchange, resulting in a ba'in divorce with proportional compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed word
The scenario: wife says 'Divorce me three times upon a thousand.' The husband gives one divorce. The pivot is the word 'ala'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa: raj'i divorce, no payment. Dalil: Quranic verse (60:12) where 'ala' indicates a condition; divorce can be suspended on conditions; the conditioned (three divorces) is indivisible from the compensation.
The Quranic verse is 'ala an la yushrikna...'
3
State the position of the two companions and Shafi'i
They say: ba'in divorce with 1/3 of the thousand. They analogize 'ala' to 'bi' in exchange contracts like sale.
The text says 'according to them and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i'
4
Analyze the key distinction: condition vs. exchange
Why does Abu Hanifa differentiate sale (cannot be conditioned) from divorce (can be)? Because in sale, conditioning nullifies the contract; in divorce, conditioning is valid. Therefore, in divorce, 'ala' retains its literal meaning of condition.
No necessity to reinterpret 'ala' as exchange.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifa: husband can take wife back (raj'i), wife pays nothing. According to the two companions: divorce is ba'in (irrevocable), wife pays 1/3 of the thousand. This affects the financial and marital status.
Also note Malik's position: full thousand due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي على أي في قولها: طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف فطلقها واحدة. مجاناً: أي لو قالت طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف، فطلقها واحدة يقع رجعيا بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن 'على' للشرط عند الإمام قال الله تعالى: ﴿ عَلَى أَنْ لا يُشْرِكْنَ بِاللَّهِ شَيْئاً ﴾ الآية، لامرأته: أنت طالق على أن تدخلي الدار يكون شرطاً، والطلاق يصح تعليقه بالشرط، والمشروط لا يتوزع على أجزاء الشرط، وههنا جاز إيقاع الثلاث شرطا للزوم الألف، والبدل لا يوزع على أجزاء الشرط، فيقع رجعية بلا شيء، وعندهما وهو قول الشافعي حاله يقع تطليقة بائنة بثلث الألف؛ لأن 'على' مثل الباء في المعاوضات عبداً بألف أو على ألف وله أن البيع لا يصح تعليقه بالشرط، فيحمل على العوض ضرورة، ولا ضرورة في الطلاق لصحة تعليقه بالشرط، فإذا لم يجب المال كان الزوج مبتدئا فيقع رجعيا، وعند مالك: يلزمها كل الألف.
And regarding 'upon' – meaning in her saying: 'Divorce me three times upon a thousand,' so he divorced her once. Free of charge: meaning if she said, 'Divorce me three times upon a thousand,' and he divorced her once, it occurs as raj'i without anything according to Abu Hanifa, because 'upon' indicates a condition according to the Imam. Allah said: 'Upon that they do not associate anything with Allah' – the verse. [If he says] to his wife: 'You are divorced upon your entering the house,' it is a condition, and divorce can be suspended on a condition, and the conditioned (talāq) is not distributed over parts of the condition. Here, it is permissible to make the three divorces a condition for the obligation of the thousand, and the compensation is not distributed over parts of the condition, so it occurs as raj'i without anything. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is the saying of al-Shafi‘i, it occurs as one ba'in divorce with one third of the thousand, because 'upon' is like 'for' in exchanges: 'a slave for a thousand' or 'upon a thousand.' And for him (Abu Hanifa), sale cannot be suspended on a condition, so it is interpreted as compensation out of necessity, but there is no necessity in divorce because it can be suspended on a condition. So when money is not obligatory, the husband is initiating, so it occurs as raj'i. According to Malik: the entire thousand is obligated on her.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where the wife says, 'Divorce me three times upon a thousand dirhams' (using the word 'ala' meaning 'upon') instead of 'for a thousand' (bi'). The ruling differs based on the nature of the word 'ala'. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'ala' indicates a condition (shart), not a direct exchange. He cites the Quranic verse where 'ala' is used for a condition. Since divorce can be suspended on a condition, and the condition (three divorces) does not divide into parts with respect to the compensation, if the husband gives only one divorce, no compensation is due, and the divorce is raj'i (revocable). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i consider 'ala' equivalent to 'bi' in exchange contracts, so it implies a direct exchange; therefore, if only one divorce is given, the wife owes one third of the thousand and the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable). Imam Malik says she owes the entire thousand. The practical consequence is whether the wife pays and whether the husband can take her back.

Key Terms
على (‘alā): Preposition meaning 'upon' used here to indicate a condition in a divorce request.
رجعي (raj‘ī): Revocable divorce; the husband can take the wife back during the 'iddah without a new marriage.
شرط (shart): Condition; here the three divorces are made a condition for the payment of the thousand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The divorce is ba'in and she owes one third of a thousand.
B) The divorce is raj'i and she owes nothing.
C) The divorce is raj'i but she owes the full thousand.
D) The divorce is ba'in and she owes the full thousand.

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'ala' in a khul' request can indicate either a condition or an exchange, leading to different rulings on revocability and payment.

Conditional Khul' with Acceptance: Partial Performance and Direct Offer
Explains rulings on khul' conditional on wife's acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling for khul' when the husband or wife conditions the divorce on payment, distinguishing when partial acceptance is valid.

Core Idea
In khul', the offeror's condition controls partial performance; acceptance in session creates a binding exchange.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains two critical rulings in khul' (divorce initiated by wife with payment). First, when a husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand' and she divorces only once, the divorce is invalid because the husband only consented to full separation. However, if the wife makes the same request and the husband divorces once, it is valid. Second, when the husband directly says 'You are divorced for one thousand' and the wife accepts in the session, the divorce becomes binding and she owes the money. Understanding these nuances prevents common errors in khul' transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: (A) offering a conditional divorce with a specific number and (B) offering a conditional divorce directly with payment. Note who initiates the offer in each case.
Scenario A distinguishes between husband-initiated and wife-initiated offers.
2
Analyze the condition of full payment
In scenario A when the husband offers, he only consents to divorce if all three divorces occur for the full thousand. Partial payment would harm him, so no divorce occurs. Contrast this with when the wife offers: she consented to full payment, so partial is acceptable.
The key is who bears the harm from partial performance.
3
Understand the role of acceptance
In scenario B, the husband states 'You are divorced for one thousand' which is a conditional offer. The wife must accept in the same session for the divorce to occur and the money to be owed. Without acceptance, nothing happens.
Acceptance is a condition because it is an exchange contract.
4
Note the legal nature of khul'
The commentary explains that khul' is an exchange (mubadala) or a conditional statement, so it requires mutual consent. The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) because she pays for her release.
This distinguishes khul' from regular divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يقع شيء، أنت طالق بألف أو على فقبلت لزمها وبانت. لم يقع شيء: الأنه لم يرض بالبينونة إلا بسلامة الألف كلها له (ع)] أي إذا قال لها الزوج: طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً بألف، فطلقت واحدة لم يقع الطلاق بثلث الألف؛ لأن الزوج ما رضي بالبينونة إلا بتسليم الألف كلها له، بوقوع الواحدة بثلث الألف كان إعجافاً وإضراراً في حقه، بخلاف قولها: "طلقني ثلاثاً بألف" فطلقها واحدة؛ لأنها لما رضيت بالبينونة بألف فلأن ترضى بها بثلث الألف أولى. (مستخلص) فقبلت لزمها وبانت [بواحدة لوجوب المال، ولو لم تقبل لا تطلق ولا يلزمها شيء. (ط،ع)] أي إذا قال لها: أنت طالق بألف أو على ألف، فقبلت المرأة طلّقت ولزمها الألف؛ لأنه مبادلة أو تعليق، فيقتضي سلامة البدل أو وجود الشرط إذا قبلت في المجلس ولم تكن مكرهة ولا سفيهة ولا مريضة وإنما قيد بقبولها؛ لأنها لو لم تقبل لا تطلق ولا يلزمها شيء، ولابد من القبول في الوجهين أعني فيما إذا قال: "بألف أو على ألف"؛ لأن معنى قوله: "بألف" بعوض يجب لي عليك، والعوض لا يجب بدون قبوله وإنما توقف الوقوع على القبول؛ لأن المعلق بالشرط لا ينزل قبل وجود الشرط، والطلاق بائن؛ لأنها لا تسلم المال له إلا أن تسلم لها نفسها. (مستخلص، فتح)
Nothing occurs. [If he says] 'You are divorced for one thousand' or 'for one thousand', and she accepts, it becomes binding and she becomes irrevocably divorced. Nothing occurs: because he did not consent to separation except with the payment of the entire thousand to him. That is, if the husband says to her: 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand', and she divorces once, the divorce does not occur for one-third of the thousand; because the husband did not consent to separation except with the full thousand given to him; by the occurrence of one divorce for one-third of the thousand, that would be unfair and harmful to him. Unlike her saying: 'Divorce me three times for one thousand', and he divorces her once; because since she consented to separation for one thousand, it is more appropriate that she consents to it for one-third of the thousand. (Mukhtasar) 'Then she accepts, it becomes binding and she becomes irrevocably divorced for one, due to the obligation of the money. If she does not accept, she is not divorced and nothing is owed.' That is, if he says to her: 'You are divorced for one thousand' or 'for one thousand', and the woman accepts, she is divorced and the thousand is required; because it is an exchange or a conditional statement, so it requires the soundness of the substitute or the occurrence of the condition when she accepts in the session, and she is not coerced, foolish, or ill. The condition of her acceptance is specified because if she does not accept, she is not divorced and nothing is owed. Acceptance is necessary in both cases, i.e., when he says 'for one thousand' or 'for one thousand'; because the meaning of his saying 'for one thousand' is 'in exchange for a consideration required for me upon you', and consideration is not obligatory without acceptance. The occurrence [of divorce] is suspended upon acceptance because a conditional event does not occur before the condition exists. The divorce is irrevocable (ba'in) because she does not surrender the money to him except that he surrenders herself to her. (Mukhtasar, Fath)
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its commentary. It addresses two scenarios in khul'. First, if the husband says to his wife 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand' and she divorces only once, no divorce occurs because the husband only intended separation if the full thousand is paid; partial payment would harm him. However, if the wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand' and the husband divorces her once, it is valid because she consented to separation for a full thousand, so she willingly accepts a lesser payment. Second, if the husband says 'You are divorced for one thousand' or 'on one thousand' and the wife accepts in the session, she is divorced (ba'in) and must pay the amount. Acceptance is essential; without it, no divorce or obligation arises. The commentary specifies that the wife must not be coerced, foolish, or ill. The divorce is ba'in because it is an exchange—she pays money for her release.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): A divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for payment (fidyah) to the husband.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return except through a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand' and she divorces once, what happens according to the Hanafi school?
A) She is divorced once and owes one-third of the thousand.
B) No divorce occurs because the husband did not consent to partial payment.
C) She is divorced three times and owes the full thousand.
D) The divorce is valid but no payment is due.

Key Takeaway
In khul', acceptance is a condition for the exchange; partial performance is valid only if it does not harm the offeror.

You are divorced and you owe a thousand: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Contingency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce with payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the phrase 'you are divorced and you owe a thousand', explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.

Core Idea
Divorce can be unconditional or conditional on payment — the Hanafi school splits on whether such a phrase creates an exchange.

This passage examines a common scenario where a husband says 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand' to his wife. Does the divorce take effect immediately, or is it contingent upon the wife's acceptance of the payment? The Hanafi imams differ, and the ruling has practical consequences for couples.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and positions
Read the passage and identify the specific statement ('you are divorced and you owe a thousand') and the two Hanafi positions: Abu Hanifa's immediate divorce with no payment vs. the Sahibayn's contingent divorce with payment upon acceptance.
The same applies to a slave with 'you are free and you owe a thousand'.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Note that Abu Hanifa treats each clause as independent: 'you are divorced' and 'you owe a thousand' are separate. Divorce can exist without monetary consideration, so no link is presumed.
The principle is 'asl inqiraad kull jumla binafsiha' (each sentence stands alone).
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn see the phrase as indicating an exchange (mu'awadah). They analogize to 'carry this food and you have a dirham', where the payment is linked to the act. Acceptance of the offer is required; if accepted, the payment is due.
Contextual indication (dalālat al-ḥāl) overrides the default independence.
4
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: If the wife does not accept, according to Abu Hanifa the divorce still occurs, but according to the Sahibayn it does not. This affects marital status and financial obligations.
The same applies when the wife says 'Divorce me and you have a thousand' — here the husband must accept.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The passage states 'biqawlihim yufti' (fatwa is given by their saying), meaning the Sahibayn's position is the relied-upone one in the Hanafi school for fatwa.
Thus, in practice, the divorce is conditional on acceptance and payment is due upon acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أنت: أي أو قال المولى لعبده أنت إلخ. (ع) طلقت وعتق مجاناً: [ أي بغير شيء سواء قبل الألف أولا. (ط)] أي إذا قال الزوج لامرأته: أنت طالق وعليك ألف أو قال السيد لعبده : أنت حر وعليك ألف طلقت المرأة بغير شيء، وعتق العبد كذلك عند أبي حنيفة سواء قبلا الألف أو لم يقبلا وعندهما على كل واحد منهما الألف إذا قبل، وان لم يقبل لا يقع الطلاق والعتاق؛ لأن هذا الكلام يستعمل للمعاوضة، فإن قولهم: "احمل هذا الطعام ولك درهم" بمنزلة قولهم: "بدرهم ، وله : أن الأصل انفراد كل جملة بنفسها، والاتصال بدلالة عارضة، وعليك ألف جملة تامة، فلا ترتبط بما قبله إلا بدلالة الحال، ولا دلالة هنا؛ لأن الطلاق والعتاق ينفكان عن المال، بخلاف البيع والإجارة فإنهما لا يوجدان بدون المال وبقولهما يفتي وبه قالت الثلاثة وعلى هذه الخلاف لو قالت المرأة لزوجها: طلقني ولك ألف"، وقال العبد: أعتقني ولك "ألف" فقبل ذلك وقع الطلاق والعتاق ولم يجب المال عليهما عنده، وعند الصاحبين يجب عليهما المال. عيني، مستخلص، فتح)
Or 'you': that is, if the master says to his slave 'you are free' etc. 'Divorced and freed free of charge': i.e., without anything, whether they accept the thousand or not. That is, if the husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand' or the master says to his slave: 'You are free and you owe a thousand', the woman is divorced without anything, and the slave is freed likewise according to Abu Hanifa, whether they accept the thousand or not. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), each of them owes a thousand if they accept, and if they do not accept, the divorce and emancipation do not occur. Because this speech is used for exchange; for their saying 'Carry this food and you have a dirham' is like saying 'for a dirham'. And for him [Abu Hanifa]: The principle is that each sentence is independent, and connection is by incidental indication, and 'you owe a thousand' is a complete sentence, so it does not connect to what precedes it except by contextual indication, and there is no such indication here because divorce and emancipation can exist without money, unlike sale and lease which cannot exist without money. And by their saying (the two) the fatwa is given, and the three (imams) said this. And on this difference, if the woman says to her husband 'Divorce me and you have a thousand', and the slave says 'Free me and you have a thousand', and he accepts, the divorce and emancipation occur and the money is not obligatory upon them according to him (Abu Hanifa), but according to the two, the money is obligatory upon them. (Ayni, Mukhtasar, Fath)
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand' (or similarly for a slave: 'You are free and you owe a thousand'). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the divorce or emancipation occurs immediately without any payment, regardless of the other party's acceptance. The statement is treated as two independent sentences: 'you are divorced/free' and 'you owe a thousand' – the debt does not connect to the divorce/emancipation. In contrast, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it an exchange; the divorce/emancipation is contingent upon acceptance of the payment. If the wife or slave accepts, they must pay; if not, no divorce/emancipation occurs. The fatwa is given according to the Sahibayn. Similarly, if the wife says 'Divorce me and you have a thousand', Abu Hanifa rules that no payment is due, while the Sahibayn require payment. The reasoning is that divorce and emancipation can exist without monetary consideration, unlike sale or lease.

Key Terms
معاوضة (mu'awadah): An exchange transaction where one thing is given in return for another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand'?
A) The divorce occurs immediately and no payment is due.
B) The divorce occurs only if she accepts, and then she must pay.
C) The divorce does not occur at all.
D) She must pay even without acceptance.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi is that 'you are divorced and you owe a thousand' is an exchange: the divorce occurs only if the wife accepts, and then she must pay.
