وبطنها وفخذها وفرجها كظهرها وأخته وعمته وأمه رضاعاً كأمه، ورأسك وفرجك ووجهك ونصفك وثلثك كأنت. وبطنها إلخ : أي لو قال لها: "أنت علي كبطن أمي أو كفخذها أو كفرجها" فهو كما لو قال: "أنت علي كظهر أمي"؛ لأن الظهار ليس إلا بتشبيه المحللة بالمحرمة، وهذا المعنى يتحقق في هذه الأعضاء وفي عضو لا يجوز النظر إليه، فيكون مظاهراً. كظهرها: أي كظهر الأم حتى لو شبه امرأته بعضو من هذه الأعضاء يكون مظاهراً؛ لأن هذه الأعضاء يحرم عليه النظر إليها ولمسها، بخلاف اليد ونحوها؛ لأنه يجوز النظر إليها ولمسها بلا شهوة. وأخته إلخ: أي إذ أشبه المظاهر امرأته بأخته أو عمته أو أمه من الرضاع أو بمن لا يحل النظر إليها على التأبيد من محارمه، فهو كما شبهها بأمه نسباً ويصير مظاهراً؛ لأن حرمتهن على التأبيد، والتقييد بأخته وعمته للاحتراز عما لو شبهها بأختها أو عمتها أو خالتها؛ لأن حرمتهن ليس على التأبيد، وإنما تحرم عليه ما دامت هي في نكاحه؛ لأجل الجمع، فإذا طلقها أو ماتت حلت له؛ لعدم الجمع. رضاعاً أي من جهة الرضاع وهو راجع إلى الكل. كأمه: نسباً حتى يصير مظاهراً بتشبيه منكوحته بواحدة منهن. ورأسك إلخ: أي إذا قال: "رأسك علي كظهر أمي" وكذا فرجك ووجهك وكذا لو قال: "نصفك علي كظهر أمي"؛ لأن هذه الأعضاء يعبر بها عن جميع البدن ويثبت الحكم في النصف والثلث أي الجزء الشائع، ثم يتعدى كما في الطلاق، ورأسك في محل الرفع على الابتداء والتقدير، وقوله : "رأسك" وقوله كـ "أنت" في محل الرفع على الخبرية أي كقوله: "أنت علي كظهر أمي". وثلثك: علي كظهر أمي كان مظاهراً في الكل.
And her belly, her thigh, and her private part are like her back, and his sister, his paternal aunt, and his milk-mother are like his mother, and your head, your private part, your face, your half, and your third are like 'you'. And 'her belly etc.': meaning if he said to her: 'You are to me like the belly of my mother or like her thigh or like her private part,' it is as if he said: 'You are to me like the back of my mother'; because zihar is nothing but likening a permissible woman to a prohibited one, and this meaning is realized in these body parts and in any part it is not permissible to look at, so he becomes a muthahir. 'Like her back': meaning like the back of the mother, so that if he likened his wife to any of these body parts, he becomes a muthahir; because these body parts are prohibited for him to look at and touch, unlike the hand and the like; because it is permissible to look at them and touch them without desire. 'And his sister etc.': meaning when the muthahir likens his wife to his sister or his paternal aunt or his milk-mother or any of his female relatives whom it is not permissible to look at permanently, then it is as if he likened her to his mother by lineage and he becomes a muthahir; because their prohibition is permanent, and the restriction to his sister and paternal aunt is to exclude the case where he likens her to her own sister or her paternal aunt or her maternal aunt; because their prohibition is not permanent, rather they are only prohibited to him as long as she is in his marriage due to the combination, so if he divorces her or she dies, she becomes permissible to him due to the absence of the combination. 'Through breastfeeding': meaning through the channel of breastfeeding, and it refers to all. 'Like his mother': by lineage, so that he becomes a muthahir by likening his wife to one of them. 'And your head etc.': meaning if he said: 'Your head is to me like the back of my mother,' and similarly her private part and her face, and similarly if he said: 'Your half is to me like the back of my mother'; because these body parts are used to refer to the entire body, and the ruling is established for a half and a third, i.e., the undivided part, then it extends as in divorce, and 'your head' is in the nominative case as the subject, and his saying 'your head' and his saying 'like you' is in the nominative case as the predicate, i.e., like his saying: 'You are to me like the back of my mother.' 'And your third': if he says: 'Your third is to me like the back of my mother,' he becomes a muthahir in all.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of valid zihar expressions. Zihar occurs when a husband likens his wife to a permanently prohibited relative, such as his mother. The ruling establishes that using specific body parts like the belly, thigh, or private part is equivalent to saying 'like the back of my mother' because the essence of zihar is likening a lawful wife to an unlawful woman, and these parts are unlawful to see and touch. Similarly, likening her to a sister, paternal aunt, or milk-mother counts as zihar because they are permanently prohibited. If he likens her to her own sister or aunt, it is not zihar because the prohibition is temporary (only during marriage). When he says 'your head,' 'your private part,' 'your face,' 'your half,' or 'your third,' the ruling applies to the entire body, as these parts represent the whole. The commentary explains that the ruling extends to undivided parts like half and third by analogy (qiyas) to divorce.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a man says to his wife 'You are to me like my mother's back,' which in Islam incurs a prohibition and requires expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following comparisons does NOT constitute zihar?
A) Saying 'you are like my sister'
B) Saying 'your head is like my mother's head'
C) Saying 'your hand is like my mother's hand'
D) Saying 'your private part is like my mother's private part'

Key Takeaway
Zihar is established by likening the wife to any permanently prohibited body part or relative, including belly, thigh, private part, sister, paternal aunt, and milk-mother, and expressions like 'head' or 'half' extend to the whole body.

Kinayah Zihar: The Phrase 'Anti Alayya Mithl Ummi' and the Role of Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the first kinayah phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for the kinayah phrase 'anti alayya mithl ummi' and explain how intention determines its legal effect according to Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Kinayah in zihar requires intention; without it, Abu Hanifa rules void, Muhammad rules zihar, and Abu Yusuf differentiates based on context.

Zihar can be pronounced explicitly (sarih) or implicitly (kinayah). The kinayah phrase 'you are to me like my mother' is inherently ambiguous—it could be intended as honor, zihar, or divorce. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute over this specific phrase and the legal weight of intention in determining its effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ambiguity
Recognise that 'anti alayya mithl ummi' does not explicitly mention 'back' or 'zihar'; it is kinayah. The same words could mean honor (karama), zihar, or divorce.
This ambiguity is the root of the dispute.
2
State the three Imams' positions
Memorise each position: Abu Hanifa says void (laghw) if no intention; Muhammad says it is always zihar; Abu Yusuf says it depends on anger – if angry, it is zihar (or ila' in one narration).
Note Abu Yusuf's alternative view of ila'.
3
Understand the evidence (dalil) for each position
Abu Hanifa: the word can be for honor (common usage), so without intention it is laghw. Muhammad: likening to the whole includes all parts, so it is automatically zihar. Abu Yusuf: anger shows intent, otherwise it can be ila' due to the text on mother's prohibition.
Each imam uses a different method of interpretation.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon view is Muhammad's: the phrase results in zihar even if no intention is expressed. This is the position used for fatwa.
This is the outcome for modern practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن نوى بـ أنت على مثل أمي برا أو ظهاراً أو طلاقاً فكما نوى وإلا لغا، وبـ " أنت علي حرام كأمي" ظهاراً أي أو نوى أي أو نوى أي مثل ما نوى المظاهر کلامه عند الشيخين أو طلاقاً فكما نوى، وبــ أنت علي حرام كظهر أمي" طلاقاً أو إيلاء فظهار، ..... أي أو نوى أي فهو ظهار فكما نوى أي لو قال لامرأته: "أنت" علي مثل أمي" يرجع إلى نية لينكشف حكمه، فإن قال: أردت به الكرامة فلا يكون مظاهراً؛ لأن التكريم بالتشبيه فاش في الكلام، وإن قال أردت الظهار فهو ظهار؛ لأنه شبهها بجميعها وفي ذلك تشبيه بعضو منها إلا أنه غير صريح، فيقتصر على النية وإن قال أردت الطلاق فهو طلاق بائن؛ لأنه تشبيه بالأم في الحرمة، فكأنه قال: أنت علي حرام ونوى الطلاق. وإلا لغا: أي إن لم ينو شيئا بقوله: "أنت علي مثل أمي" لغا كلامه؛ لأنه محمل في حق التشبيه فما لم يتبين مراداً مخصوصاً لا يحكم بشيء، وعند محمد: هو ظهار؛ لأنه شبهها بجميعها، فيدخل العضو في الجملة، وعن أبي يوسف مثله إذا كان حالة الغضب، وعنه أنه يكون إيلاء؛ لأن أمه محرمة عليه بالنص، فيحمل عليه؛ لأن الحرام يمين بالنص، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه يمكن حملها على الكرامة، فلا ضرورة في جعلها ظهاراً، ولو قال: "أنت علي كأمي" فهو مثل قوله: "أنت" علي مثل أمي" في الوجوه كلها. وبأنت علي حرام إلخ أي إذا قال: أنت علي حرام كأمي ونوى ظهاراً أو طلاقاً فهو كما نوى؛ لأن قوله: "أنت علي حرام" من الكنايات فيكون طلاقاً بالنية، وقوله: "كأمي" لتأكيد تلك الحرمة، فلا يخرج به من أن يكون طلاقاً، وإن نوى بهذا القول الظهار فظهار؛ لأنه شبهها في الحرمة بأمه فلو شبهها بظهرها كان ظهاراً فبكلها أولى، وانتفى احتمال البر والكرامة في هذه المسألة؛ لتصريحه بالحرمة، وإن لم يكن له نية فهو ظهار؛ لأنه لفظ محتمل فيثبت به الأدنى، وعند أبي يوسف: هو إيلاء؛ ليكون الثابت به أدنى الحرمات؛ لأن سبب الإيلاء وحكمه أخف، ويمكن رفعه بالوطء ولا يبقى حكمه بعد زوج آخر. نوى وإن لم يكن له نية فظهار عند محمد وهو الصحيح. فظهار: أي لو قال: "أنت علي حرام كظهر أمي" يكون ظهاراً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن هذا اللفظ صريح في الظهار؛ لأن معنى قوله "أنت علي حرام كظهر أمي": أنت على كظهر أمي، فيكون لفظ الحرام تفسيرا للظهار، فلا تعمل فيه النية؛ لأن الشيء لا يتغير بتفسيره، وقالا : إن نوى ظهاراً أو لم تكن له نية فهو ظهار، وإن نوى طلاقاً فطلاق، وإن نوى إيلاء فإيلاء؛ لأن كلاً منها محتمل لكلامه؛ إذ التحريم يحتمل كل ذلك، ثم إن نوى الطلاق لا يكون ظهاراً عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف: يكونان معاً، الظهار بلفظه، والطلاق بنيته، وإن نوى إيلاء ينبغي أن يكون إيلاء وظهاراً باتفاقهما.
And if he intends by 'you are to me like my mother' [honor] or zihar or divorce, then it is as he intends, otherwise it is void. And by 'you are to me forbidden like my mother' it is zihar, or if he intends divorce then as he intends. And by 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother' it is divorce or ila' it is zihar... meaning if he said to his wife: 'you are to me like my mother,' it refers to his intention to reveal its ruling. If he said: I meant by it honor, then he is not a muthahir; because honoring by likening is common in speech. If he said: I intended zihar, then it is zihar; because he likened her to her entirety and that includes likening a part of her, except it is not explicit, so it is confined to intention. If he said: I intended divorce, then it is a bain divorce; because it is likening to the mother in prohibition, so it is as if he said: you are forbidden to me and intends divorce. 'Otherwise it is void': meaning if he did not intend anything by saying 'you are to me like my mother,' his speech is void; because it is ambivalent regarding likening, so as long as a specific meaning is not clarified, nothing is judged. According to Muhammad: it is zihar; because he likened her to her entirety, so the part is included in the whole. And from Abu Yusuf a similar view if he is in a state of anger, and from him that it is ila'; because his mother is prohibited to him by text, so it is carried upon that; because the forbidden is an oath by text. And for Abu Hanifa: that it can be carried upon honor, so there is no necessity to make it zihar. And if he said: 'you are to me like my mother,' it is like his saying 'you are to me like my mother' in all aspects. 'And by you are to me forbidden etc.': meaning if he said: 'you are to me forbidden like my mother' and intended zihar or divorce, then it is as he intended; because his saying 'you are to me forbidden' is from the kinayah, so it becomes divorce by intention, and his saying 'like my mother' is to emphasize that prohibition, so it does not remove it from being divorce. And if he intended by this statement zihar, then it is zihar; because he likened her in prohibition to his mother, so if he if he likened her to her back it would be zihar, then likening to her entirety is more appropriate. And the possibility of honor and respect is negated in this issue because he explicitly stated prohibition. And if he had no intention, then it is zihar; because the word is probable, so the lesser [meaning] is established. According to Abu Yusuf: it is ila'; so that the established [rule] is the lighter of prohibitions; because the cause and ruling of ila' are lighter, and it can be removed by intercourse and its ruling does not remain after another husband. 'He intends and if he had no intention, then it is zihar according to Muhammad, and it is the correct [view].' 'So it is zihar': meaning if he said: 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother,' it is zihar according to Abu Hanifa; because this wording is explicit in zihar; because the meaning of his saying 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother' is 'you are to me like the back of my mother,' so the word 'forbidden' is an explanation of zihar, so intention does not affect it; because a thing is not changed by its explanation. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if he intends zihar or has no intention, then it is zihar; and if he intends divorce, then it is divorce; and if he intends ila', then it is ila'; because each of them is possible from his statement, since prohibition can possibly mean all of that. Then if he intends divorce, it is not zihar according to Muhammad; and according to Abu Yusuf, both apply: zihar by its wording, and divorce by his intention. And if he intends ila', it should be ila' and zihar by their agreement.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses the kinayah (implicit) expressions of zihar. The central issue is that phrases like 'You are to me like my mother' or 'You are to me forbidden like my mother' are not explicit (sarih) in zihar; they can carry other meanings like honor, divorce, or ila'. Therefore, the ruling depends on the speaker's intention (niyyah). The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that such phrases are ambivalent and if no intention is stated, the speech is void (laghw). Muhammad holds that it is zihar because the likening to the entire mother implies all parts. Abu Yusuf has a conditional view. The phrase 'You are to me forbidden like my mother' adds the explicit word 'haram,' which negates the possibility of honor, so it becomes either zihar or divorce by intention; if no intention, it is zihar according to Muhammad and the relied-upon view. For the phrase 'You are to me forbidden like the back of my mother,' Abu Hanifa considers it explicit zihar because 'haram' explains zihar, so intention does not change it. The Sahibayn hold that it is subject to intention and can become divorce or ila'. The passage reveals the nuanced methodology of Hanafi fiqh: words are classified into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit), with kinayah requiring niyyah to determine the legal effect.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An implicit expression whose intended meaning is not clear from the words alone and requires intention (niyyah) to determine its legal effect.
لغو (laghw): Void speech that has no legal effect because no valid intention is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view, if a husband says 'You are to me like my mother' with no intention and no context, what is the ruling?
A) It is void (laghw).
B) It is zihar.
C) It is a bain divorce.
D) It is ila'.

Key Takeaway
For the kinayah phrase 'anti alayya mithl ummi', the relied-upon Hanafi ruling is that it constitutes zihar even without explicit intention, per Imam Muhammad.

Kinayah Zihar: The Phrase 'Haram Ka-Ummi' and 'Haram Ka-Dhahri Ummi' – Intention and Explicit Zihar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on two haram-related zihar phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for the phrases 'anti alayya haram ka-ummi' and 'anti alayya haram ka-dhahri ummi', and explain the role of intention in each.

Core Idea
Adding 'haram' to 'like my mother' removes the possibility of honor, but the ruling differs whether the back is specified or not.

When a husband adds the word 'haram' (forbidden) to the likening, the ambiguity changes. The phrase 'you are to me forbidden like my mother' makes it clear that honor is not intended, but it can still mean zihar, divorce, or ila' depending on intention. However, if he says 'forbidden like the back of my mother', Abu Hanifa considers it explicit zihar, while the Sahibayn still require intention. This lesson dissects these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases and their differences
The first phrase is 'anti alayya haram ka-ummi' (forbidden like my mother). The second is 'anti alayya haram ka-dhahri ummi' (forbidden like the back of my mother). Note that the second specifies a body part.
The word 'haram' negates the possibility of honor.
2
State the ruling for 'haram ka-ummi'
If he intends zihar, it is zihar; if he intends divorce, it is divorce (bain). If no intention, the relied-upon view (Muhammad) is that it is zihar. Abu Yusuf says it is ila'.
No void option here because 'haram' removes honor.
3
State the dispute for 'haram ka-dhahri ummi'
Abu Hanifa says it is explicit (sarih) zihar; intention is irrelevant. The Sahibayn say it is kinayah and can be zihar, divorce, or ila' by intention.
This is a major intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the evidence for each position on the second phrase
Abu Hanifa: 'haram' explains the meaning of zihar; since the back is mentioned, it clarifies that zihar is intended. The Sahibayn: 'haram' is general and can be for divorce or ila'.
The key is whether 'haram' clarifies or remains ambiguous.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for both phrases
For 'haram ka-ummi', the mufta bihi is zihar if no intention. For 'haram ka-dhahri ummi', the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view: explicit zihar, intention irrelevant.
Both are used in practice.

Source Text

كظهر أمي" طلاقاً أو إيلاء فظهار، ..... أي أو نوى أي فهو ظهار فكما نوى أي لو قال لامرأته: "أنت" علي مثل أمي" يرجع إلى نية لينكشف حكمه، فإن قال: أردت به الكرامة فلا يكون مظاهراً؛ لأن التكريم بالتشبيه علي حرام ونوى الطلاق. وإلا لغا: أي إن لم ينو شيئا بقوله: "أنت علي مثل أمي" لغا كلامه؛ لأنه محمل في حق التشبيه فما لم يتبين مراداً مخصوصاً لا يحكم بشيء، وعند محمد: هو ظهار؛ لأنه شبهها بجميعها، فيدخل العضو في الجملة، وعن أبي يوسف مثله إذا كان حالة الغضب، وعنه أنه يكون إيلاء؛ لأن أمه محرمة عليه بالنص، فيحمل عليه؛ لأن الحرام يمين بالنص، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه يمكن حملها على الكرامة، فلا ضرورة في جعلها ظهاراً، ولو قال: "أنت علي كأمي" فهو مثل قوله: "أنت" علي مثل أمي" في الوجوه كلها. وبأنت علي حرام إلخ أي إذا قال: أنت علي حرام كأمي ونوى ظهاراً أو طلاقاً فهو كما نوى؛ لأن قوله: "أنت علي حرام" من الكنايات فيكون طلاقاً بالنية، وقوله: "كأمي" لتأكيد تلك الحرمة، فلا يخرج به من أن يكون طلاقاً، وإن نوى بهذا القول الظهار فظهار؛ لأنه شبهها في الحرمة بأمه فلو شبهها بظهرها كان ظهاراً فبكلها أولى، وانتفى احتمال البر والكرامة في هذه المسألة؛ لتصريحه بالحرمة، وإن لم يكن له نية فهو ظهار؛ لأنه لفظ محتمل فيثبت به الأدنى، وعند أبي يوسف: هو إيلاء؛ ليكون الثابت به أدنى الحرمات؛ لأن سبب الإيلاء وحكمه أخف، ويمكن رفعه بالوطء ولا يبقى حكمه بعد زوج آخر. نوى وإن لم يكن له نية فظهار عند محمد وهو الصحيح. فظهار: أي لو قال: "أنت علي حرام كظهر أمي" يكون ظهاراً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن هذا اللفظ صريح في الظهار؛ لأن معنى قوله "أنت علي حرام كظهر أمي": أنت على كظهر أمي، فيكون لفظ الحرام تفسيرا للظهار، فلا تعمل فيه النية؛ لأن الشيء لا يتغير بتفسيره، وقالا : إن نوى ظهاراً أو لم تكن له نية فهو ظهار، وإن نوى طلاقاً فطلاق، وإن نوى إيلاء فإيلاء؛ لأن كلاً منها محتمل لكلامه؛ إذ التحريم يحتمل كل ذلك، ثم إن نوى الطلاق لا يكون ظهاراً عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف: يكونان معاً، الظهار بلفظه، والطلاق بنيته، وإن نوى إيلاء ينبغي أن يكون إيلاء وظهاراً باتفاقهما.
And if he intends by 'you are to me like my mother' [honor] or zihar or divorce, then it is as he intends, otherwise it is void. And by 'you are to me forbidden like my mother' it is zihar, or if he intends divorce then as he intends. And by 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother' it is divorce or ila' it is zihar... meaning if he said to his wife: 'you are to me like my mother,' it refers to his intention to reveal its ruling. If he said: I meant by it honor, then he is not a muthahir; because honoring by likening is common in speech. If he said: I intended zihar, then it is zihar; because he likened her to her entirety and that includes likening a part of her, except it is not explicit, so it is confined to intention. If he said: I intended divorce, then it is a bain divorce; because it is likening to the mother in prohibition, so it is as if he said: you are forbidden to me and intends divorce. 'Otherwise it is void': meaning if he did not intend anything by saying 'you are to me like my mother,' his speech is void; because it is ambivalent regarding likening, so as long as a specific meaning is not clarified, nothing is judged. According to Muhammad: it is zihar; because he likened her to her entirety, so the part is included in the whole. And from Abu Yusuf a similar view if he is in a state of anger, and from him that it is ila'; because his mother is prohibited to him by text, so it is carried upon that; because the forbidden is an oath by text. And for Abu Hanifa: that it can be carried upon honor, so there is no necessity to make it zihar. And if he said: 'you are to me like my mother,' it is like his saying 'you are to me like my mother' in all aspects. 'And by you are to me forbidden etc.': meaning if he said: 'you are to me forbidden like my mother' and intended zihar or divorce, then it is as he intended; because his saying 'you are to me forbidden' is from the kinayah, so it becomes divorce by intention, and his saying 'like my mother' is to emphasize that prohibition, so it does not remove it from being divorce. And if he intended by this statement zihar, then it is zihar; because he likened her in prohibition to his mother, so if he if he likened her to her back it would be zihar, then likening to her entirety is more appropriate. And the possibility of honor and respect is negated in this issue because he explicitly stated prohibition. And if he had no intention, then it is zihar; because the word is probable, so the lesser [meaning] is established. According to Abu Yusuf: it is ila'; so that the established [rule] is the lighter of prohibitions; because the cause and ruling of ila' are lighter, and it can be removed by intercourse and its ruling does not remain after another husband. 'He intends and if he had no intention, then it is zihar according to Muhammad, and it is the correct [view].' 'So it is zihar': meaning if he said: 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother,' it is zihar according to Abu Hanifa; because this wording is explicit in zihar; because the meaning of his saying 'you are to me forbidden like the back of my mother' is 'you are to me like the back of my mother,' so the word 'forbidden' is an explanation of zihar, so intention does not affect it; because a thing is not changed by its explanation. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if he intends zihar or has no intention, then it is zihar; and if he intends divorce, then it is divorce; and if he intends ila', then it is ila'; because each of them is possible from his statement, since prohibition can possibly mean all of that. Then if he intends divorce, it is not zihar according to Muhammad; and according to Abu Yusuf, both apply: zihar by its wording, and divorce by his intention. And if he intends ila', it should be ila' and zihar by their agreement.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses the kinayah (implicit) expressions of zihar. The central issue is that phrases like 'You are to me like my mother' or 'You are to me forbidden like my mother' are not explicit (sarih) in zihar; they can carry other meanings like honor, divorce, or ila'. Therefore, the ruling depends on the speaker's intention (niyyah). The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that such phrases are ambivalent and if no intention is stated, the speech is void (laghw). Muhammad holds that it is zihar because the likening to the entire mother implies all parts. Abu Yusuf has a conditional view. The phrase 'You are to me forbidden like my mother' adds the explicit word 'haram,' which negates the possibility of honor, so it becomes either zihar or divorce by intention; if no intention, it is zihar according to Muhammad and the relied-upon view. For the phrase 'You are to me forbidden like the back of my mother,' Abu Hanifa considers it explicit zihar because 'haram' explains zihar, so intention does not change it. The Sahibayn hold that it is subject to intention and can become divorce or ila'. The passage reveals the nuanced methodology of Hanafi fiqh: words are classified into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit), with kinayah requiring niyyah to determine the legal effect.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): An explicit expression whose meaning is clear and does not require intention to take legal effect.
حرمة (hurma): Sanctity/prohibition; here it refers to the forbidden relationship with mahram relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi, what is the ruling for the phrase 'anti alayya haram ka-dhahri ummi' if the husband has no intention?
A) It is zihar, and intention cannot change it.
B) It is void because intention is missing.
C) It is a bain divorce because 'haram' implies divorce.
D) It is ila' because 'haram' implies an oath.

Key Takeaway
Adding 'haram' does not automatically make a phrase explicit; only specifying the back (or similar) does. For 'haram ka-dhahri ummi', Abu Hanifa's view (explicit zihar) is relied upon for fatwa.

Zihar Utterances: Sarih, Kinayah, and the Necessity of Niyyah
Explaining legal effect of zihar-like phrases and necessity of niyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a zihar-related utterance as sarih or kinayah and state the corresponding ruling on intention according to each Hanafi imam (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).

Core Idea
Sarih zihar needs no niyyah; kinayah zihar requires it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq delves into the nuanced classification of zihar pronouncements. While the Quran mentions the explicit phrase 'ka'zahri ummi' (like the back of my mother), Muslims may use other similar expressions. The Hanafi school distinguishes between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (ambiguous) wording, with intention (niyyah) playing a decisive role in the latter. However, the three founding imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—disagree on which phrases are sarih and what happens when intention is absent or differs. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the rules of zihar in fatwa and daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
Read the exact phrase the husband said. Note whether it contains the word 'zahr' (back) or 'haram' (forbidden), and whether it includes a comparison to the mother or another relative.
2
Classify as sarih or kinayah per Abu Hanifa
Apply Abu Hanifa's classification: If the phrase mirrors the Quranic wording 'ka'zahri ummi' (like the back of my mother), it is sarih. If it is a comparison without 'zahr' (e.g., 'like my mother'), it is kinayah.
3
Check the Sahibayn's alternative classification
Recall that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad may classify even 'haram kazahri ummi' as kinayah, giving intention a role. Note their specific positions on each phrase.
4
Determine the effect of intention (or its absence)
For kinayah phrases, ask: Did the husband intend zihar, talaq, ila', or nothing? The ruling changes accordingly. For sarih, intention is irrelevant and the phrase is automatically zihar.
5
Apply the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling
Check which of the three imam's views is considered sahih (sound) for that specific phrase. The commentary indicates that for 'haram ka'ummi' without intention, Muhammad's view (it is zihar) is correct.

Source Text

Chapter of Zihar. Or zihar or talaq, then it is according to his intention, otherwise it is void. And with "You are forbidden to me like my mother" as zihar... or as talaq, then as he intended. And with "You are forbidden to me like the back of my mother" as talaq or ila', then it is zihar. ... then as he intended. Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq (with commentary) discusses the legal effects of various pronouncements that may constitute zihar, focusing on the role of intention (niyyah). The text begins by stating the general rule: if a man says something ambiguous that could be zihar or talaq, it takes effect according to his intention; if he has no intention, the utterance is void. The commentary then examines specific phrases. For the phrase "anti 'alayya mithlu ummi" (you are like my mother), Abu Hanifa holds that it is not explicit; intention is required to determine whether it is zihar, talaq, or mere honor. If he intends honor, it is void. Muhammad says it is automatically zihar because comparing her to the whole implies comparing to a part. Abu Yusuf says it is zihar if said in anger, otherwise ila'. For "anti 'alayya ka'ummi" (you are like my mother), the same rulings apply. For "anti 'alayya haram ka'ummi" (you are forbidden to me like my mother), this phrase is kinayah; it becomes talaq with intention to divorce, or zihar with intention to zihar, and without intention it is zihar (Muhammad's position, deemed sahih). Abu Yusuf considers it ila' without intention. For "anti 'alayya haram kazahri ummi" (you are forbidden to me like the back of my mother), Abu Hanifa considers this explicit (sarih) zihar because the phrase 'like the back of my mother' is the original wording of zihar in the Quran, and the word 'haram' only interprets it, so intention does not change it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is not explicit; intention matters, and if he intends talaq or ila', those can apply. Within the Sahibayn, Muhammad holds that if he intends talaq, the zihar is not valid; Abu Yusuf says both take effect. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) seems to be Muhammad's view on several points, as indicated by 'huwa al-sahih'. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of classifying zihar utterances into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (metaphorical), with different rules for each.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A pronouncement comparing one's wife to a prohibited relative (like mother) in terms of marital intimacy, which triggers a temporary prohibition requiring kaffarah before resuming relations.
نية (niyyah): Intentional determination that converts an ambiguous (kinayah) utterance into a specific legal effect (zihar, talaq, ila').
صريح (sarih): Explicit wording that directly indicates zihar without need for intention; e.g., 'anti 'alayya kazahri ummi' according to Abu Hanifa.
كناية (kinayah): Metaphorical or ambiguous wording that requires intention to determine its legal effect; e.g., 'anti 'alayya haram ka'ummi' is kinayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a man says to his wife 'anti 'alayya haram kazahri ummi' without any specific intention?
A) It is zihar, because the phrasing is explicit.
B) It is ila', because of the prohibition.
C) It is talaq ba'in, because of the comparison.
D) It is void, because no intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, classification of zihar utterances into sarih and kinayah determines whether intention is required; intra-madhhab differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad must be noted for correct fatwa.

Zihar Is Only from a Wife: The Hanafi Position and Its Proof
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that zihar is only valid from a wife, explain the Quranic proof, and rebut the Maliki view that includes slave-girls.

Core Idea
Zihar is only from a wife, not a slave-girl – the Quranic 'nisaihim' means wives.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational condition for zihar: it applies only to one's wife under a valid marriage. The Hanafi school bases this on the Quranic phrase 'from their women' (min nisa'ihim), interpreted as wives. We will also address the case where a woman ratifies a marriage concluded without her permission, and the dispute with Imam Malik concerning slave-girls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states: 'There is no zihar except from his wife.' Determine what zihar is and what the ruling implies for its validity.
Zihar is a prohibited form of divorce-like speech, making marital relations temporarily forbidden until expiation.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites the verse 'And those who make zihar from their women' (Quran 58:3). Analyze why the term 'nisaihim' is interpreted as wives, not slave-girls.
The addition of the pronoun 'him' (husbands) limits the meaning to women in a marital relationship.
3
Understand the rationale for excluding slave-girls
The text explains that in a slave-girl, the primary purpose is service, not permissible intercourse (halal). Therefore, she is not a locus for the prohibition that expiation removes.
This reasoning uses istihsan and the nature of ownership versus marriage.
4
Consider the Maliki objection and Hanafi response
Imam Malik holds that zihar can be from a slave-girl. The Hanafi response is based on the Quranic text and the analogy: since a slave-girl is not a locus for divorce, she cannot be a locus for zihar (which originally was a form of divorce).
The thamarah al-khilaf: in the Maliki school, a man would owe expiation for zihar on a slave-girl; in the Hanafi school, no expiation.
5
Note the additional condition: marriage without permission
If a man marries a woman without her permission and then makes zihar, but she later ratifies the marriage, the zihar becomes void. This is because at the time of utterance the marriage was not valid.
This reinforces the principle that zihar must be from a valid wife at the moment of utterance.

علي كظهر أمي" ظهار منهن، وكفر لكل.

النكاح
ولا ظهار حرة كانت أو مملوكة مسلمة أو كتابية، فلو ظاهر من أمته لا يكون مظاهراً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُظَاهِرُونَ مِنْ نِسَائِهِمْ الآية (الممتحنة:٣) ولفظ النساء يتناول المنكوحات؛ إذ لفظ النساء مضافاً إلى الأزواج لا يتناول الإماء، ولأن الحل ليس بمقصود في الأمة، وإنما المقصود الاستخدام حتى يثبت ملك اليمين في من لا يحل له وطئها، فلا تكون مقصودة بالتحريم؛ إذ الحل فيها تبع لملك اليمين لا مقصود، وفي المنكوحة أصل فيمتنع الإلحاق خلافاً لمالك، والنص حجة عليه؛ لأن الظهار كان طلاقاً في الجاهلية، فنقل الشرع حكمه إلى تحريم مؤقت بالكفارة، والأمة ليست بمحل للطلاق، فلا تكون محلاً للظهار كالإيلاء كان طلاقا في الحال، فأخره الشرع إلى مضي أربعة أشهر. رمز الحقائق: ۲۸۳/۱] (فتح)
بطل: [ أي الظهار؛ لأنه صادق في التشبيه في ذلك الوقت، فلا يجب عليه جزاء الزور. (ع)] هذه المسألة تفريع لقوله: "لا ظهار إلا من زوجته" أي من نكح امرأة بلا إذنها فظاهر منها عقيب النكاح، فأجازت المرأة النكاح بعد ما بلغه الخبر وبعد ما ظاهر منها بطل الظهار؛ لأنه صادق في التشبيه وقت التشبيه و لم يتوقف على الإجازة كالنكاح، لأنه ليس من حقوقه. (مستخلص فتح أنتن أي قول الرجل لزوجاته: أنتن إلخ. (ط) ظهار منهن جميعا، وارتفاع ظهار على أنه خبر القول المقدر. (ع)] لوجود ركنه في كلهن وهو التشبيه؛ لأنه أضاف الظهار إليهن، فصار كما إذا أضاف إليهن الطلاق، ولو كرّر الظهار من واحدة في مجلس تكررت الكفارة عليه إلا إذا نوى التأكيد بالثاني فيصدق قضاء. (فتح) وكفر لكل [ أي المظاهر في المسألة المذكورة. (ع)] أي كفر المظاهر لكل واحدة منهن، فإن كن ثلاثاً فثلاث كفارات، وإن كانت أربعاً فأربع؛ لأنه لو آلى منهن كان عليه كفارة واحدة والفرق أنها في الظهار لانتهاء الحرمة وهي تثبت في حق الكل، فتتعدد الكفارة بتعددها، وعند مالك يكفيه كفارة واحدة إذا ظاهر منهن بكلمة واحدة كما لو قال لهن: "والله لا أقربكن" ثم قربهن لم يلزمه إلا كفارة واحدة، ولنا: أن الكفارة لانتهاء الحرمة وهي تثبت في حق كل واحدة منهن، فتتعدد بتعددهن بخلاف الإيلاء؛ لأن الكفارة تجب فيه لهتك حرمة
اسم الله تعالى، وهو الواحد فلا تتعدد إلا بتعدد ذكر اسم الله عزوجل. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٨٤/١] (فتح)
Zihar. And there is no zihar except from his wife. So if he marries a woman without her command and then makes zihar from her, and she ratifies it, it becomes void. 'You (feminine plural) are upon me like the back of my mother' is zihar from them, and expiation for each. (i.e., without her permission) (i.e., for each one of them) The marriage. And there is no zihar, whether she is free or slave, Muslim or kitabi. So if he makes zihar from his slave-girl, he is not a muzahir due to the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And those who make zihar from their wives...' (al-Mumtahanah 3) and the word 'women' refers to those married; because the word 'women' when added to 'husbands' does not include slave-girls. And because permissible intercourse is not intended in the slave-girl; rather the intended is utilization until ownership of the right hand is established in one whose intercourse is not permissible for him, so she is not intended for prohibition; since permissibility in her is subsidiary to ownership of the right hand, not intended. And in the married woman, it is original, so analogy is prevented, contrary to Malik. The text is proof against him; because zihar was a divorce in the Jahiliyyah, then the Shariah transferred its ruling to a temporary prohibition with expiation. And the slave-girl is not a locus for divorce, so she is not a locus for zihar, just as ila' was a divorce immediately, then the Shariah delayed it to the passage of four months. (Ramooz al-Haqa'iq 1/283) (Fath) 'Batala' (becomes void): i.e., the zihar; because he was truthful in the comparison at that time, so he is not obliged a penalty for falsehood. (Ayni) This issue is a branch of his saying 'There is no zihar except from his wife': i.e., if he marries a woman without her permission and makes zihar from her immediately after the marriage, and the woman ratifies the marriage after the news reaches him and after he made zihar from her, the zihar becomes void; because he was truthful in the comparison at the time of comparison and it was not suspended on ratification like the marriage, because it is not among its rights. (Mukhtalas, Fath) 'Antunna' i.e., the saying of a man to his wives: 'Antunna...' etc. (T) 'Ziharun minhunna jami'an' (zihar from all of them), and the elevation of zihar as the predicate of the estimated saying. (Ayni) Due to the existence of its pillar in all of them, which is the comparison; because he attributed the zihar to them, so it becomes as if he attributed divorce to them. And if he repeated the zihar from one in one sitting, multiple expiations become obligatory upon him, except if he intends confirmation by the second, then he is believed judicially. (Fath) 'Wa kaffarun li kull' (and expiation for each): i.e., the expiation of the muzahir in the mentioned issue for each one of them. So if they are three, then three expiations; if four, then four; because if he made ila' from them, he would have one expiation, and the difference is that in zihar it is for the termination of prohibition which is established in the right of each, so the expiation multiplies by their multiplicity. According to Malik, one expiation suffices him if he makes zihar from them with one word, just as if he said to them: 'By Allah, I will not approach you' then he approached them, only one expiation is required. And we (Hanafis) hold: that the expiation is for the termination of prohibition, which is established in the right of each one of them, so it multiplies by their multiplicity, contrary to ila'; because the expiation in it is obligatory for violating the sanctity of the name of Allah the Exalted, which is one, so it does not multiply except by multiplicity of mentioning the name of Allah. [Ramooz al-Haqa'iq 1/284] (Fath)
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage establishes the conditions for the validity of zihar. First, zihar only applies from a wife (zawjah) under a valid marriage contract. If a man marries a woman without her permission (i.e., her guardian did not authorize) and then pronounces zihar, and later the woman ratifies the marriage, the zihar is void because at the time of utterance the marriage was not validly established. Second, zihar is not valid from a slave-girl (amah), even if she is the man's property. The proof is Quran 58:3 which uses the term 'nisaihim' (their women) which in context means wives, not slave-girls. Additionally, the wisdom is that in a slave-girl, the primary purpose is service (istikdam), not sexual enjoyment; permissible intercourse is subsidiary to ownership, not the primary aim. Zihar was originally a form of divorce in pre-Islamic times, and the Shariah replaced it with a temporary prohibition requiring expiation. Since a slave-girl is not a locus for divorce, she is not a locus for zihar. This is a point of disagreement with Imam Malik. Then the passage discusses the scenario of multiple wives. If a man says to his wives 'antunna 'alayya kazahri ummi' (you all are like the back of my mother to me), it constitutes zihar for each wife, requiring separate expiation for each. The reason is that the prohibition is established for each wife individually, so expiation is needed for each. This differs from ila' (vow of abstinence), where the expiation is for violating the sanctity of Allah's name, which is singular, so only one expiation is required regardless of the number of wives, unless Allah's name is repeated. The passage also notes a disagreement with Malik, who holds that one expiation suffices for all wives if the zihar is pronounced in one word. The Hanafi argument emphasizes the multiplicities of prohibitions (hurmah) for each wife.

Key Terms
زوجة (zawjah): A woman under a valid marriage contract (nikah); the only subject from whom zihar is valid according to this text.
أمة (amah): A female slave; zihar from her is invalid because she is not a 'wife' and the primary purpose is service, not marital relations.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for zihar before resuming marital relations; in the case of multiple wives, multiple kaffarat are due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man makes zihar from his female slave, what is the ruling?
A) Valid zihar, requiring expiation.
B) Invalid zihar, no expiation required.
C) Valid only if the slave is Muslim.
D) Considered as a form of freeing the slave.

Key Takeaway
Zihar is only valid from a wife under a valid marriage; it does not apply to slave-girls.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Zihar on Multiple Wives: Separate Expiation for Each
Inter-madhhab dispute on multiple expiations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that zihar pronounced on multiple wives in one sentence requires separate expiation for each, and explain the distinction from ila'.

Core Idea
Zihar on multiple wives multiplies expiation because each wife's prohibition is separate; ila' does not.

This lesson focuses on a second condition: if a man says to all his wives in one sentence, 'You are to me like my mother's back,' the Hanafi school holds that each wife is individually prohibited, requiring separate expiations. We will compare this with ila' and with the Maliki position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses a man who says to his multiple wives, 'antunna 'alayya kazahri ummi' (you all are like the back of my mother). Recognize that this is a single utterance addressed to all of them.
The utterance 'antunna' is feminine plural, indicating all wives are addressed.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The text says 'zihar from them all, and expiation for each.' So the ruling is that multiple expiations are required, one for each wife.
If there are three wives, three expiations; if four, four expiations.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reason is that the prohibition (hurmah) that zihar creates applies to each wife individually. The expiation is for lifting that prohibition. Since each wife has her own prohibition, lifting each requires its own expiation.
This is contrasted with ila' (vow of abstinence) where the expiation is for violating the sanctity of Allah's name, which is one, so it does not multiply.
4
Compare with the Maliki view
Imam Malik holds that one expiation suffices for all if said in one word, analogizing to ila'. The Hanafi response differentiates the basis of the expiation: in zihar it is based on the prohibition attached to each wife, in ila' it is based on the name of Allah.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in number of expiations owed.
5
Apply the ruling to a repeated zihar from one wife
A separate sub-rule: if a man repeats zihar to one wife in one sitting, multiple expiations are due unless he intends confirmation (tawkid), in which case he is believed judicially.
This shows that the principle of multiplicity applies even to the same wife when the statement is repeated.

علي كظهر أمي" ظهار منهن، وكفر لكل.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A man saying to his wife 'You are like the back of my mother' or similar, which creates a temporary prohibition requiring expiation.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow of abstinence from marital relations for four months; if not resumed, divorce occurs.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for zihar before resuming marital relations; in the case of multiple wives, multiple kaffarat are due.

Zihar Is Only Valid from a Wife
Ruling with proof that Zihar applies only to wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zihar from a slave-girl and explain the Quranic evidence and analogy with divorce that support it.

Core Idea
Zihar only applies to a wife, not a slave-girl, because the Quranic 'nisa'ihim' means wives and slave-girls are not subject to divorce.

This lesson clarifies a key condition of zihar: it must be directed at a wife. The Hanafi school derives this from the Quranic phrase and the legal nature of slave-girls. We will examine the proof, a practical scenario, and a dispute with Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
State the ruling: Zihar is only valid when addressed to a wife, whether free or slave, Muslim or Kitabiyya. It is not valid for a slave-girl (concubine).
The ruling is a condition (shart) for the zihar to have legal effect.
2
Examine the Quranic Dalil
The Quran (58:3) uses the phrase 'min nisa'ihim' (from their women). In Arabic, when 'nisa'' (women) is added to a pronoun indicating possession by a husband, it specifically means wives.
This linguistic point is critical: idhafa al-nisa' ila al-zawj takhuss bi al-zawjat.
3
Understand the Rationale
The purpose of owning a slave-girl is service, not lawful intercourse. The halal in her is subordinate to ownership. In contrast, a wife's primary right is lawful intercourse, making her the proper subject of zihar.
The prohibition in zihar targets the halal relationship, which is dominant in marriage.
4
Analyze the Branch Case
If a man marries a woman without her permission and immediately pronounces zihar, then the woman ratifies the marriage later, the zihar becomes void. Reason: At the time of pronouncement, the woman was unlawful for him, so the comparison was truthful; no expiation is owed.
Zihar does not depend on ratification; it is valid or invalid based on the status at the time of utterance.
5
Review the Dispute with Malik
Imam Malik holds that zihar can apply to a slave-girl. The Hanafi response: The Quranic word 'nisa'ihim' excludes slave-girls, and zihar was originally a form of divorce; slave-girls are not subject to divorce, so they cannot be subject to zihar.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: in the Maliki school, expiation would be required; in the Hanafi, none.

كتاب الطلاق الظهار ولا ظهار إلا من زوجته فلو نكح امرأة بلا أمرها فظاهر منها فأجازته بطل، ولا ظهار حرة كانت أو مملوكة مسلمة أو كتابية، فلو ظاهر من أمته لا يكون مظاهراً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُظَاهِرُونَ مِنْ نِسَائِهِمْ الآية (الممتحنة:٣) ولفظ النساء يتناول المنكوحات؛ إذ لفظ النساء مضافاً إلى الأزواج لا يتناول الإماء، ولأن الحل ليس بمقصود في الأمة، وإنما المقصود الاستخدام حتى يثبت ملك اليمين في من لا يحل له وطئها، فلا تكون مقصودة بالتحريم؛ إذ الحل فيها تبع لملك اليمين لا مقصود، وفي المنكوحة أصل فيمتنع الإلحاق خلافاً لمالك، والنص حجة عليه؛ لأن الظهار كان طلاقاً في الجاهلية، فنقل الشرع حكمه إلى تحريم مؤقت بالكفارة، والأمة ليست بمحل للطلاق، فلا تكون محلاً للظهار كالإيلاء كان طلاقا في الحال، فأخره الشرع إلى مضي أربعة أشهر. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۸۳/۱] (فتح) بطل: [ أي الظهار؛ لأنه صادق في التشبيه في ذلك الوقت، فلا يجب عليه جزاء الزور. (ع)] هذه المسألة تفريع لقوله: "لا ظهار إلا من زوجته" أي من نكح امرأة بلا إذنها فظاهر منها عقيب النكاح، فأجازت المرأة النكاح بعد ما بلغه الخبر وبعد ما ظاهر منها بطل الظهار؛ لأنه صادق في التشبيه وقت التشبيه و لم يتوقف على الإجازة كالنكاح، لأنه ليس من حقوقه.
Book of Divorce. Zihar. And there is no zihar except from his wife. If he marries a woman without her command and does zihar from her, then she ratifies it, it is void. And there is no zihar whether she is free or slave, Muslim or Kitabiyya. If he does zihar from his slave-girl, he is not a muzzahir (one who performs zihar) because of His saying, the Exalted: 'And those who pronounce zihar from their wives...' (al-Mumtahina:3). And the word 'women' includes those who are married; for the word 'women' when added to husbands does not include slave-girls. And because (the right to) halal (intercourse) is not the intended purpose (maqsud) in a slave-girl; rather, the intended purpose is service, until ownership (milk al-yamin) is confirmed for one whom it is not permissible to have intercourse with. Thus she is not intended by prohibition; for the halal in her is subordinate to ownership, not intended. And in the married woman, it is the origin, so joining (the slave-girl to her) is prevented, unlike Malik. And the text is evidence against him. Because zihar was a divorce in the Jahiliyya, then the Lawgiver transferred its ruling to a temporary prohibition with expiation. And the slave-girl is not a place for divorce, so she is not a place for zihar, like ila: it was immediate divorce, then the Lawgiver delayed it to the passing of four months. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/283] (Fath) 'Batal' (void): [i.e., the zihar; because he was truthful in the comparison at that time, so he is not obligated to pay a penalty for falsehood. (Ayni)] This issue is a branch of his saying: 'There is no zihar except from his wife'; i.e., he married a woman without her permission and did zihar from her immediately after the marriage, then the woman ratified the marriage after the news reached him and after he had done zihar from her, the zihar is void; because he was truthful in the comparison at the time of comparison and it did not depend on ratification like the marriage, because it is not one of its rights.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This lesson is a ruling with proof. The matn states a fundamental rule: Zihar (the pronouncement 'You are to me like the back of my mother') is only valid when directed at one's wife. The commentary (sharh) expands on this, providing evidence and a related case. The ruling: Zihar applies only to a wife, whether she is free or slave, Muslim or Kitabiyya (Scriptuary). It does not apply to a concubine (umm walad or mere slave-girl). The proof: Quran 58:3 (al-Mujadilah or al-Mumtahina, actually 58:2-3) uses the word 'nisa'ihim' (their women/wives). In Arabic, when the word 'nisa' is added to a masculine plural pronoun, it specifically means 'wives', not slave-girls. The commentary explains the rationale: in a slave-girl, the primary purpose of ownership is service, not lawful intercourse (halal). The halal in her is incidental to ownership. Therefore, she is not the object of prohibition through zihar. In contrast, the wife's primary purpose is lawful intercourse. The text also addresses a specific scenario: if a man marries a woman without her permission and immediately pronounces zihar, and then the woman ratifies the marriage, the zihar becomes void. Why? Because at the time of the pronouncement, he was truthful in likening her to his mother (i.e., she was haram for him at that instant since the marriage was not yet ratified). Thus, he is not liable for falsehood. The commentary further clarifies that the zihar does not depend on ratification; it is valid or void based on the status at the time. A dispute with Imam Malik is noted: Malik holds that zihar can apply to a slave-girl, but the Hanafi school rejects this, arguing that the text (Quran) and analogy with divorce (talaq) support their view. In Jahiliyya, zihar was a form of divorce; Islam changed it to a temporary prohibition requiring expiation. Since a slave-girl is not subject to divorce, she cannot be subject to zihar, just as ila (vow of abstinence) was originally immediate divorce but Islam postponed it.

Key Terms
الظهار (al-zihar): A pronouncement by a husband likening his wife to a mahram (e.g., his mother) in terms of sexual unlawfulnees, which triggers a wajib expiation before intercourse.
نسائهم (nisa'ihim): In the Quranic context, 'their women' meaning their wives (azwaj), not slave-girls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid and he must perform expiation before touching her.
B) It is not valid, and no expiation is required.
C) It is valid only if she is a Muslim.
D) It is invalid unless he also intends divorce.

Key Takeaway
Zihar is a form of prohibited speech that only triggers expiation when directed at a wife, not a slave-girl, based on Quranic usage and legal analogy.

Zihar Pronounced to Multiple Wives: Ruling on Expiations
Ruling with proof and dispute on zihar to multiple wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why zihar to multiple wives requires multiple expiations and distinguish zihar from ila in this regard.

Core Idea
One pronouncement of zihar to many wives = separate expiation for each wife, because the prohibition attaches to each individually.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a husband pronounces zihar on all his wives in one statement. Does he owe one expiation or multiple? The Hanafi school, building on the rukn of tashbih and qiyas with divorce, rules that each wife requires her own expiation. Understanding this ruling clarifies the nature of zihar's prohibition and its difference from similar institutions like ila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a man saying to his wives collectively, 'You are all like my mother's back.' The ruling is that this counts as zihar for each wife, requiring a separate expiation for each.
The pillar of zihar is the comparison (tashbih), which is present for each wife.
2
Apply qiyas with divorce
Just as a man saying 'You are all divorced' to multiple wives results in divorce for each, so too does the pronouncement of zihar take effect on each wife individually.
The effective cause ('illa) is the establishment of prohibition per wife.
3
Distinguish from ila
In ila (vow to abstain), the expiation is for violating Allah's name, which is one. In zihar, the expiation is for the prohibition specific to each wife, so it multiplies with the number of wives.
The difference lies in the reason for the expiation.
4
Address the ikhtilaf with Imam Malik
Imam Malik holds that one expiation suffices, analogizing to ila. The Hanafi response is that the analogy fails because the expiation in zihar is not for a single act but for each prohibition.
The Hanafi tarjih is that the per-wife ruling is mufta bihi.
5
Note the ruling on repetition to one wife
If a man repeats zihar to the same wife in one sitting, the expiation multiplies unless he intends the second as emphasis, in which case he is believed judicially.
This shows the principle that multiple independent pronouncements create multiple obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ع)] لوجود ركنه في كلهن وهو التشبيه؛ لأنه أضاف الظهار إليهن، فصار كما إذا أضاف إليهن الطلاق، ولو كرّر الظهار من واحدة في مجلس تكررت الكفارة عليه إلا إذا نوى التأكيد بالثاني فيصدق قضاء. (فتح) وكفر لكل [ أي المظاهر في المسألة المذكورة. (ع)] أي كفر المظاهر لكل واحدة منهن، فإن كن ثلاثاً فثلاث كفارات، وإن كانت أربعاً فأربع؛ لأنه لو آلى منهن كان عليه كفارة واحدة والفرق أنها في الظهار لانتهاء الحرمة وهي تثبت في حق الكل، فتتعدد الكفارة بتعددها، وعند مالك يكفيه كفارة واحدة إذا ظاهر منهن بكلمة واحدة كما لو قال لهن: "والله لا أقربكن" ثم قربهن لم يلزمه إلا كفارة واحدة، ولنا: أن الكفارة لانتهاء الحرمة وهي تثبت في حق كل واحدة منهن، فتتعدد بتعددهن بخلاف الإيلاء؛ لأن الكفارة تجب فيه لهتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى، وهو الواحد فلا تتعدد إلا بتعدد ذكر اسم الله عزوجل. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٨٤/١] (فتح)
'You (feminine plural)' i.e., the man's saying to his wives: 'You (all)...' [Tusi]. It is zihar from all of them, and 'zihar' is raised (in i'rab) as the predicate of the implied saying [Ayni]. Because its pillar (rukn) exists in all of them, which is the comparison; because he attributed the zihar to them, so it becomes as if he attributed divorce to them. And if he repeated zihar on one (wife) in one sitting, the expiation multiplies upon him, unless he intends the second as an emphasis, then he is believed judicially [Fath]. And expiation for each [i.e., the muzzahir in the mentioned issue (Ayni)] i.e., the muzzahir must expiate for each one of them. If they are three, then three expiations; if four, then four. Because if he had performed ila (vow of abstinence) on them, he would owe only one expiation. And the difference is that in zihar, it is for the termination of prohibition, and it is established in the right of all, so the expiation multiplies with their multiplicity. According to Malik, one expiation suffices for all if he pronounces zihar on them with one word, just as if he said to them, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then he approached them, only one expiation is due. And we hold: the expiation is for the termination of prohibition, and it is established in the right of each one of them, so it multiplies with their multiplicity, contrary to ila; because the expiation in ila is due for violating the sanctity of Allah's name, which is one, so it does not multiply except with multiple utterances of Allah's name. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/284] (Fath)
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This lesson discusses the scenario where a man pronounces zihar (e.g., 'You are like my mother's back') addressing all his wives collectively, as in 'You (feminine plural)'. The ruling from the matn is that this counts as a zihar pronouncement for each wife individually. The commentary explains why: the pillar (rukn) of zihar is the comparison (tashbih) of the wife to a mahram. Here, the man has compared each wife to his mother, so the legal effect applies to each. It is analogous to pronouncing divorce upon all wives in one statement – it takes effect on each. The discussion also clarifies the expiation required: if he says it to three wives, he owes three separate expiations (freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding). If he repeats the zihar to the same wife in one sitting, the expiation is multiplied unless he intends the second as an emphasis (ta'kid), in which case his intention is accepted in the court of law (qada'). The commentary contrasts zihar with ila (the vow to abstain from intercourse). In ila, if a man swears by Allah not to approach his wives collectively, only one expiation is due for violating the oath, because ila's expiation is for violating Allah's name, which is one. In zihar, the expiation is for the termination of the prohibition that is established individually for each wife. A disagreement with Imam Malik is recorded: Malik holds that one expiation suffices for all wives if the pronouncement is a single statement. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition (hurmah) is established for each wife separately, so the expiation must be separate.

Key Terms
التشبيه (al-tashbih): The comparison of the wife to a mahram, which is the pillar (rukn) of zihar.
الإيلاء (al-ila'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One expiation
B) Three expiations
C) None, because it was one statement
D) One expiation, but he can divide it among them

Key Takeaway
Zihar pronounced to multiple wives in one statement creates a separate prohibition for each wife, requiring a separate expiation for each.

Talaq — Divorce

Conditions of the Slave in Zihar Expiation
Conditions for the slave in zihar expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a slave to be valid in zihar expiation, apply the Hanafi principle of 'loss of the entire genus of benefit', and distinguish the Hanafi ruling from the Shafi'i position on freeing a non-Muslim slave.

Core Idea
A slave for zihar expiation must retain the full genus of each major benefit; partial defects are acceptable.

This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on which slaves are valid for the expiation (kaffarah) of zihar. The matn states that a blind slave, one missing both hands, both feet, or both thumbs, and a completely insane slave are invalid. The commentary expands by introducing the key principle: loss of the entire genus of a major benefit (sight, grip, walking, reason) renders the slave invalid, but mere defects (like one-eyed, one-handed) do not. We will also examine a significant inter-madhhab dispute regarding freeing a non-Muslim slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The matn states that it is not permissible to free a blind slave, one missing both hands or thumbs or feet, or a completely insane slave. Understand that these are the only invalid categories; all other slaves are valid.
The commentary states: 'the loss of the genus of benefit prevents, defect does not.'
2
Apply the principle of entire genus loss
For each listed defect, identify which genus of benefit is lost: sight (blind), grip (hands/thumbs), walking (feet), reason (completely mad). Then learn that minor defects like one-eyed, missing one hand or foot, or deafness (if hearing possible) are permissible because the genus is still present.
The text explicitly allows a one-eyed person and one missing a single hand or foot.
3
Analyze the dispute on religion of the slave
The Hanafi school holds that a non-Muslim slave (including kitabi and mushrik) is valid, based on the unqualified term 'raqaba' in the Quran. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools require a believing slave. Understand each side's proof and the Hanafi response.
The Hanafi proof includes Quran 60:8 about doing good to non-hostile non-Muslims.
4
Consider intra-Hanafi differences
Zufar opposed the majority on the permissibility of a castrato, a lovesick man, and an earless slave. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) allowed these because the missing benefits (reproduction, beauty) are not essential to the slave's function.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi: such slaves are permissible.
5
Evaluate the condition of sanity
A completely insane slave (who lacks reason entirely) is invalid. But a slave who alternates between sanity and insanity is valid if freed during a lucid interval, because reason is partially present.
The commentary explains that benefit from limbs requires reason, so its total absence invalidates.

فصل في بيان الكفارة وهو تحرير رقبة، ولم يجز الأعمى ومقطوع اليدين أو إبهاميهما أو الرجلين، والمجنون أي التكفير شبه من هي أي مقطوعهما فصل: أي في بيان الكفارة، وإنما يجب الكفارة للمظاهر ؛ لأنه أتى منكر من القول وزور كما هو منصوص عليه؛ لأنه في أقصى غايات الحل بمن هي في أقصى غايات الحرمة فناسب أن يجازى بالحرمة المغياة بالكفارة. وكفارة الظهار عتق رقبة، فإن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين، فإن لم يستطع فإطعام ستين مسكيناً؛ للنص الوارد فيه، فإنه يفيد الكفارة على هذا الترتيب كل ذلك قبل الوطء، وهذا في الإعتاق والصوم ظاهر؛ للتنصيص عليه، وكذا في الإطعام؛ لأن الكفارة فيه منهية للحرمة، فلابد من تقديمها على الوطء؛ ليكون الوطء حلالا . [رمز الحقائق: ٢٨٤/١] (فتح) ولم يجز الأعمى [ أي تحرير المملوك الأعمى بخلاف الأعور والأعمش. (ط)] والأصل أن فوات جنس الجواز، والاختلال لا يمنع وعلى هذا لا يجوز الأعمى؛ لفوات جنس المنفعة وهو البصر، ولا مقطوع اليدين؛ لفوات البطش، وكذا مقطوع إبهامي اليدين ولا مقطوع الرجلين؛ لفوات المشي، ويجوز الأصم الذي يسمع إذا عليه، والأعور ومقطوع إحدى اليدين وإحدى الرجلين من خلاف، أما إذا كان من جانب واحد، فإنه لا يجوز لفوات المشي عليه، ويجوز الخصي والمحبوب ومقطوع الأذنين، وقال زفر : لا يجوز هؤلاء؛ لأنهم هالكون من وجه؛ لفوات منفعة الجمال في مقطوع الأذنين والإيلاد في الخصي والمحبوب قلنا: إن هاتين المنفعتين زائدتان، ولا تصير الذات لفواتهما كالهالك كما لا تصير بفوات اللحية ،والحاجب والأخرس لا يجوز؛ لفوات جنس | وقيد بإبهامي اليدين؛ لأن مقطوع إبهامي الرجلين لا يمنع الجواز. Section on the explanation of the expiation. And it is the freeing of a slave. And it is not permissible (to free) a blind person, one whose hands or thumbs or feet are cut off, or a mad person. i.e., the expiation is a likeness of whom it is. i.e., whose two thumbs are cut off. Section: i.e., on the explanation of the expiation. And the expiation is obligatory upon the one who did zihar because he has come with an evil statement and falsehood as is stated in the text. Because he is in the ultimate degree of permissibility (with his wife) while she is in the ultimate degree of unlawfulness (as a mahram), so it is suitable that he be punished with a prohibition conditional upon expiation. And the expiation of zihar is freeing a slave. If he does not find, then fasting two consecutive months. If he is unable, then feeding sixty poor persons due to the text that has come concerning it. For it indicates the expiation in this order, all of that before intercourse. This is apparent in the case of freeing and fasting because of the explicit text, and likewise in feeding, because the expiation in it is for ending the prohibition, so it must precede intercourse to make the intercourse lawful. (Fath Mahshi) 'Freeing a slave': [before intercourse, whether the slave is male or female, young or old, Muslim or disbeliever, due to the general nature of the text. (Ayni)] The word 'raqaba' originally means the back of the neck; the slave is named by it as naming the whole by the part, whether the slave is Muslim or disbeliever due to the generality of the text. Imam al-Shafi'i said: a disbelieving slave is not permissible, as in the case of manslaughter. Malik and Ahmad also said this. Because the expiation is a right of Allah, so it should not be expended on an enemy of Allah. We say: the text states freeing a slave, and that is fulfilled. And the principle is to act upon every text with respect to its generality and restriction. And being an enemy of Allah does not prevent doing good to him, as Allah says: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you...' (al-Mumtahina: 8). An apostate male is permissible according to some, not permissible according to others because he is deserving of execution. An apostate woman is permissible without disagreement. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/284] (Fath) 'And it is not permissible (to free) a blind person' [i.e., freeing a blind slave, unlike a one-eyed or bleary-eyed person. (Tusi)] And the principle: the loss of the genus (of benefit) (prevents), but defect (without loss of genus) does not prevent. On this basis, a blind person is not permissible due to the loss of the genus of benefit, which is sight. And one whose hands are cut off (is not permissible) due to the loss of grip. Similarly, one whose thumbs of the hands are cut off, and one whose feet are cut off due to the loss of walking. A deaf person who hears when shouted at is permissible. A one-eyed person and one who has one hand or one foot cut off are permissible. If the foot is cut off from one side (i.e., entire leg), it is not permissible due to the loss of walking. A castrato (khasi), a man afflicted with a love-sickness (mahbub), and one whose ears are cut off are permissible. Zufar said: these are not permissible because they are defective in one aspect: the loss of the benefit of beauty in the earless, and the loss of reproduction in the castrato and the love-sick. We say: These two benefits are additional, and the essential nature does not become like the perished due to their loss, just as it does not become like the perished due to the loss of a beard or eyebrow. And a mute person is not permissible due to the loss of the genus of benefit. And it is restricted to the thumbs of the hands because the loss of the thumbs of the feet does not prevent validity. (Ayni, Fath) 'His two thumbs' i.e., whose two thumbs of the hands are cut off. (Ayni) 'And the mad person' is conjoined to his saying: 'the blind', i.e., it is not permissible to free a mad person who does not possess reason. If he is sane at times and insane at others, his freeing is permissible in the expiation during the period of sanity, because benefit from the limbs is only through reason. So the completely insane lacks benefit; the one who alternates benefits are defective, and defect does not prevent as was mentioned. (Mustakhlas) 'The benefit is sound' (?)
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This lesson is a ruling with proof regarding the expiation (kaffarah) of zihar. The primary expiation is freeing a slave. The text provides detailed conditions for which slaves are acceptable and which are not. The commentary explains the rationale behind each condition. The matn states: 'It is not permissible: a blind person, one whose hands or thumbs or feet are cut off, and a mad person.' The commentary expands, giving the principle: the loss of the entire genus of a major benefit (e.g., sight, grip, walking) invalidates the expiation, but mere defects (e.g., one-eyed, missing one hand or one foot) do not. This is based on the concept that the slave must be capable of performing the basic functions of a free person? Actually the focus is on utility. The commentary also addresses a dispute with Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad regarding freeing a non-Muslim slave: the Hanafi school allows it because the Quranic text says 'raqaba' without qualification. The proof text for interacting kindly with non-hostile non-Muslims is cited. Another dispute with Zufar: Zufar does not permit a castrato, a love-sick man, or an earless person, but the Hanafi school permits them because the missing benefits are not essential to the slave's function as a 'raqaba' (the whole person). The lesson also discusses the case of an apostate male (some allow, some not) and an apostate woman (unanimously allowed). Finally, the condition of sanity: a completely insane person is not permissible, but one who alternates between sanity and insanity is permissible if freed during a lucid interval. The expiation must be performed before intercourse to make it lawful.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqaba): Literally the back of the neck; metonymically a slave, whether male or female, young or old, Muslim or non-Muslim.
جنس المنفعة (jins al-manfa'ah): The genus/category of benefit; loss of the whole genus (e.g., sight, grip, walking) invalidates the slave for expiation.
فوات الجنس (fawt al-jins): The loss of the entire genus of a benefit, which prevents the validity of the slave for expiation.
أعمى (a'ma): Blind person; one who has lost sight entirely.
أعور (a'war): One-eyed person; has vision in one eye.
خصي (khasi): Castrated male slave.
محبوب (mahbub): A man afflicted with love-sickness (passionate love) that impairs health? Actually, context suggests a state where one is weak due to love; the slave is still valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following slaves is valid for the expiation of zihar?
A) A blind slave
B) A slave missing both hands
C) A one-eyed slave
D) A completely insane slave

Key Takeaway
A slave for zihar expiation is valid only if it retains the complete genus of major benefits (sight, grip, walking, reason); partial defects and difference of religion (Hanafi) do not invalidate.

Conditions of the Slave in Zihar Kaffarah
Ruling with proof: conditions of valid slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid slave in zihar kaffarah according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish which defects invalidate the slave and which do not, with the textual evidence.

Core Idea
Only complete loss of a major benefit invalidates a slave for kaffarah; mere deficiency does not.

The expiation (kaffarah) for zihar requires freeing a slave before marital relations can resume. But not every slave qualifies: the slave must be free from defects that prevent full utilisation. This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the Hanafi position on valid and invalid slaves, citing Quranic proof and responding to objections from other schools and even from within the Hanafi school (Zufar). Understanding these conditions ensures that the expiation is legally effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic requirement
The kaffarah is freeing a slave. Determine the basic qualifications: according to the text, the slave can be male or female, young or old, Muslim or non-Muslim due to the absolute wording of the Quran.
The absolute term 'raqabah' includes all these categories.
2
Understand the principle of defects
The key rule: complete loss of a major benefit invalidates, but mere deficiency does not. Identify which defects are considered complete loss.
Examples: blindness (loss of sight), loss of both hands (loss of grip), both feet (loss of walking), permanent insanity (loss of reason).
3
Apply the principle to specific cases
Work through the examples given in the text: blind (invalid), one-eyed (valid), both hands cut (invalid), one hand cut from different sides (valid), both feet cut (invalid), deaf (valid), eunuch (valid), permanently insane (invalid), intermittent insanity (valid during lucidity).
The principle is applied consistently.
4
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i position that non-Muslim slaves are invalid is presented. Study the Hanafi response using Quran 60:8 and the absolute text.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue with thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi allows non-Muslim slaves, others require believing slaves.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar
Zufar argues that eunuchs, 'mahbub', and ear-cut slaves are invalid because they lack beauty or procreation. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) respond that these are additional benefits; loss does not make the slave 'perishing'. The majority view is mufta bihi.
The Hanafi school follows the majority view, rejecting Zufar's stricter position.

(ط)] والأصل أن فوات جنس الجواز، والاختلال لا يمنع وعلى هذا لا يجوز الأعمى؛ لفوات جنس المنفعة وهو البصر، ولا مقطوع اليدين؛ لفوات البطش، وكذا مقطوع إبهامي اليدين ولا مقطوع الرجلين؛ لفوات المشي، ويجوز الأصم الذي يسمع إذا عليه، والأعور ومقطوع إحدى اليدين وإحدى الرجلين من خلاف، أما إذا كان من جانب واحد، فإنه لا يجوز لفوات المشي عليه، ويجوز الخصي والمحبوب ومقطوع الأذنين، وقال زفر : لا يجوز هؤلاء؛ لأنهم هالكون من وجه؛ لفوات منفعة الجمال في مقطوع الأذنين والإيلاد في الخصي والمحبوب قلنا: إن هاتين المنفعتين زائدتان، ولا تصير الذات لفواتهما كالهالك كما لا تصير بفوات اللحية، والحاجب والأخرس لا يجوز؛ لفوات جنس | وقيد بإبهامي اليدين؛ لأن مقطوع إبهامي الرجلين لا يمنع الجواز. Section on the explanation of the expiation: it is freeing a slave. And it is not valid for the blind, one with both hands cut off or both thumbs or both feet, and the insane. The expiation is obligatory for the one who makes zihar because he uttered an abominable speech and falsehood as is textual. The expiation of zihar is freeing a slave; if he does not find, then fasting two consecutive months; if he cannot, then feeding sixty poor persons; based on the text, it indicates the expiation in this order, all before intercourse. As for freeing and fasting, it is apparent because they are explicitly mentioned; likewise feeding because the expiation necessitates prohibition, so it must precede intercourse for it to be lawful. (Fath with gloss) Freeing a slave: before intercourse, whether the slave is male or female, young or old, Muslim or disbeliever due to the absolute text. The word 'raqabah' originally means the nape, used for the slave by naming the whole after the part. It is valid whether the slave is Muslim or disbeliever due to the absolute text. Al-Shafi'i said: a disbeliever is not valid as in the expiation for accidental killing; Malik and Ahmad also said this because the expiation is a right of Allah so it should not be spent on an enemy of Allah. We say: the text specifies freeing a slave and that is accomplished; the principle is to apply each text absolutely and restrictively, and being an enemy of Allah does not prevent doing good to him as Allah says: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you' (Quran 60:8). An apostate (male) is valid according to some, not according to others because he deserves death, while an apostate woman is valid without dispute. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/284) (Fath) And it is not valid for the blind: i.e., freeing a blind slave, unlike a one-eyed or weak-sighted. The principle is that loss of the type of benefit prevents, but deficiency does not. On this basis, the blind is not valid due to loss of the type of benefit which is sight; nor the one with both hands cut off due to loss of grip; likewise both thumbs cut off; nor the one with both feet cut off due to loss of walking. The deaf who hears when addressed is valid, the one-eyed, the one with one hand or one foot cut off from different sides; but if both are from one side, it is not valid due to loss of walking. The eunuch, the beloved (effeminate?), and the one with ears cut off are valid. Zufar said: these are not valid because they are perishing in a sense due to loss of beauty in the earless and procreation in the eunuch and beloved. We say: these two benefits are additional, and the essence does not become perishing with their loss just as it does not with the loss of beard. The eyebrow? and the mute are not valid due to loss of the type of benefit. And it is restricted to both thumbs of the hands; because losing both thumbs of the feet does not prevent validity. (Ayni, Fath) 'Ibhaimayhima' means both thumbs of the hands. And 'the insane' is coordinated with 'the blind', i.e., freeing an insane person who does not reason is not valid; but if he becomes insane intermittently, freeing him during sanity is valid because the use of limbs depends on reason; the insane who does not reason has lost benefits, the one who alternates has deficient benefits, and deficiency is not a preventer as mentioned. (Mustakhlas)
